PITMAN’S SHORTHAND BOOKS.
Every enterprising man catches short-
hand, like measles, at a certain stage of
growth ; and Pitman’s is, on the whole, the
best system to catch. It is simple, scientific,
and reliable.
The books recommended to the student on
commencing the study of Phonetic Short-
hand are the “Phonographic Teacher,”
“Key,” and “Progressive Studies,” or
“Pitman’s Shorthand Instructor” and
“ Key.” The art may be learned from these
books without the aid of a personal teacher.

To Correspondents,— EKve: “A First
Lesson in French ” can be posted you for 3s.
4d., or “The Art of Teaching and Studying
Languages ” for Bs. The former gives simply
an outline of the larger work. It is a book for
teachers, not for students; and the method
seems fantastic, though suggestive. It doesn’t
profess to teach French—only how to teach
French ~.W. J. : The best “ Tristram
Shandy ” is that just published in 2 vols. of
Methuen’s “English Classics” (posted 4s,
each). The books are admirably turned out
with clear type and wide margins, bound in
buckram Darwinist: Kidd’s “ Social Evolu*
tion ” is the latest contribution to pseudo-
science-suggestive, certainly; but merely a
dogmatic deduction from an unproven a priori.
Price, 12s. 9d. posted—and not worth it.
Better wait a few months for a cheaper edition.

A facetious settler at Toonabuc (Vic.),
being asked wherein Govt, had failed to meet the
wants of the village-settlers, pointed out that it
had neglected to provide an undertaker.
Orchardson’s “ The First Cloud,” pur -
chased for the Vic. National Gallery for <£2750,
seems to have been only a replica. If so, there is
ground for a hearty protest against the artist or
the London committee, inasmuch as these gentle-
men were badly taken in if they didn’t recklessly
pay a false price for the picture. “The First
Cloud ” is not a mighty work either in conception
orexecution—-twoordinary modern “society” figures
occupying an immense tract of drawing-room
interior—and a replica of it couldn’t be worth
anything like the sum aforesaid.
All over Australia, Judges’ associates have
a good time. These gentry, taking Victoria as a
•ample, draw J£3oo a year each, their duties being
to sit in front of the Judge and cancel the filing-
stamps on affidavits and other documents. There
is no training required—the duties can be learned
in a day. Besides the regular salary, all associates
are commissioners for receiving affidavits, and
receive about .£3 a week from this source. Then
they get huge fees for making (or rather sweating
the miserable clerks who make) copies of their
Judges’ notes of evidence. Their hours (when it
is not a holiday) are 10 to 4; all their exs. are
paid on circuit, and altogether an associate’s billet
is a rosy one. As an instance that merit has
nothing to do with their appointment, it is suffi-
cient to mention that the associates to four of the
Victorian Judges are the Judges’ sons.

When the Duke of Edinburgh was in
Melb., he stated, according to the Argus, that the
crowd welcoming him was the largest assemblage
he had ever seen, with the exception of those of
the Princess Alexandra’s entry to London and the
Duke of Wellington’s funeral. As a matter of
fact, Edinburgh was absent from England on each
of these occasions.
During a recent earthquake in Italy 60
people fled into a church to pray, but the earth-
quake destroyed the church and buried the
worshippers in the ruins. It is under such pain-
ful circumstances that the Presbyterian Assembly
“sees regret the spread of modern indif-
ferentism/and lays stress upon the great necessity
for encouraging tinner faith in a personal deity.”
In face of the evidence now extant, Annie
Besant’s defence of Mrs. Mellon, on the grounds
that she herself witnessed no frauds and that
under some circumstances allowance should be
made for hysterical mediums who commit frauds,
stamps the apostle of Theosophy as either unwill-
ing to admit facts which tell against her theories,
or as incapable of estimating the value of proof.
Cardinal Moran is an attentive reader of
the sermons preached by all sorts of Protestants,
and scissors into a neatly-kept scrap-book passages
w hich strike him as of future controversial value.
Enthusiasts also send him cuttings dealing with
doctrinal matter, with their own comments, some-
times at essay length, on the views propounded.
Hardly anything new, even new stupidities, can
be written on such subjects ; but to the Cardinal
the themes are aM-absorbing,
“I think that in the land-tax alone \ ®
have the task before us of winning back for the
State, either by taxation or in specie, the land of
which the State has been wrongfully deprived. I
think that we have, and not far off from us,
the national duty of regulating, and, indeed,
entirely altering, the whole system of taxation,
and of instituting a system by which acquired
property shall bear the greater bulk of the national
burthens. ”— Higinbotham.

London Sun lately printed a poem as
by Oscar Wilde, and next week it found out he
had nothing to do with the authorship, whereupon
the editor accused him of plagiarism. Oscar arose
in wrath and denounced this exhibition of the
“coarse humor and coarser malice that are, by the
contempt of all, conceded to the ordinary jour-
nalist.” The Sun made a weak explanation, and
added to the offence by saying the editor had
thought the poem a relic of Oscar’s “ forgotten
and generous youth.” Then Oscar closed the dis-
cussion with the remark that such thoughts
“ showed a lamentable ignorance of the nature of
the artistic temperament. Only mediocrities pro-
gress, An artist revolves in a cycle of master-
pieces, the first of which is no less perfect than
the last.” Which magnificent utterance knocked
the Sun right out.

The little maiden who is full of romances
concerning love’s young dream should seriously
consider the tale of a married pair, as told lately
in Sydney divorce-court. The lady, whose front
name was Mary Ann, wedded at the age of 14
years and 8 months, and the rest of her story goes
into a few words. The pair quarrelled for 24
years and then He cleared out. They bad
nine children, who all grew up to be navvies. The
mother alternately attended a railway-crossing
and kept a boarding-house. Also He turned
up occasionally for 14 years more and raised Cain,
and only went away again when there was no
more Cain left to elevate. Think of the vari-
ous elements in that romance: He —his wife
Mary Hann—24 years dish-throwing—l 4 years
running a boarding-house—a family of nine navvies
—and at last a plain, stout, honest old boarding-
house keeper, environed by an odor of fried steak,
applying for a divorce. Apparently there is a
material gehenna, after all.
NO LAND-TAX!
** O, spare our land!” the Fat Men groan—
“ Tax anything but ‘Thrift; ’
Retrench until you bare the bone,
And let deficits drift.
The democrat of justice prates ;
He does not understand
What harm is done to large estates
By taxes on the land.
“ Send civil servants off to dig
In barren spots remote ;
Reduce—begad ! it’s far too big—
The education-vote;
Tax incomes, if it must be done,
Though with a gentle hand ;
But please respect the squatter’s run
Ar.d do not tax the land.
“ Consider, too, the farmer poor,
A good old ‘ gag ’ is he ;
* The wolf is howling at his door ’ —
That wolf’s the mortgagee.
’Tis true he doesn’t make a fuss,
Or sympathy demand ;
But spare hitn, for the sake of us,
And do not tax the land.”

Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have Tub Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.

to he addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

“Correspondence" column all contributions submitted
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable).
Paragraphs , Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 8, 1894.
A Public Works Policy.
It may be accepted as a matter proved
beyond dispute that the hot’ > i has fallen—
for the time, at all events—o .; of the Public
Works policy of all the older provinces of
Australasia. With the exception of Westra-
lia, which is only just starting on the same
giddy race as its predecessors, all the States
of the continent and the adjacent islands are
suffering from severe financial stringency.
There have been great and wonderful chances
within the last half-century, such chances as
no people on this earth—not even the gaudy
conquerors of Mexico and Peru—ever en-
joyed within historical times, and they have
been remorselessly squandered by those
grand old pioneers whose memories Austra-
lians of to-day are still taught to reverence.
There were immense revenues to be derived
in the early days from the goldfields and
from the sale of a virgin continent—enough
to build all the public works which could be
required in three generations ; and there
were big surpluses in the Treasury,
and the pioneers fooled them all away.
They ate up all the available capital in the
present, and inaugurated a loan policy by
which a national railway system was built—
largely for their benefit—and the bill handed
down to posterity. Most of that system
cost twice its real value, because of the vast
expense involved in buying back land which
the pioneers had sold to themselves for a
»ong in former days, and much of the money
required to buy it back was borrowed at
exorbitant interest from foreign usurers.
And for these and many other reasons Aus-
tralia owes to these rude forefathers of the
land a debt which can’t be expressed on
their tombst mes without speaking evil of
the dead a d using unseemly language con-
cerning their bones.
* • •
The present situation is the natural result
of the policy adopted in the past. In Vic-
toria, Queensland, and New South Wales
all the more immediately profitable railway
lines are already built, and involve a heavy
annual loss to the revenue. The lines
which are now required—if built on the
same old plan, with money borrowed
in England at heavy interest and mostly
fooled away in paying enormous prices
for resumed land—would probably increase
that annual loss until a financial collapse
became inevitable. Meanwhile, the almost
entire stoppage of public works has thrown a
large number of people out of employment
and increased the general distress, and with
the increase of distress the public revenues
have dried up, and thus the evil has reacted
upon itself and multiplied at compound in-
terest Therefore, the great questions
among the stereotyped financiers of Aus-
tralia are now, first, how to raise more money
for railway construction at a sufficiently low
rate of interest ; and, second, how to devise
some method whereby lines can be built so
cheaply that they will pay working expenses
and interest in remote and barren regions
while those in more fertile and better popu-
lated localities fail to do so, and yet freights
and fares can be made so low that settlement
will be possible. It is sufficiently evident
that none of these things can be accom-
plished by the methods now in use, and to the
politicians who exist in the rut of old tradition
no other methods seem possible. Yet The
Bulletin firmly believes that other methods
are possible and even easy. The result is not
to be arrived at by building light lines which
will need constant repair, or narrow gauge
lines which will need entire reconstruc-
tion in the near future, nor by “ private
enterprise,” either under the land-grant or
any other system. The engineering methods
now in vogue are mostly good enough. It
is the financial methods which require entire
reorganisation.
•• • .
One of the heaviest items in railway con-
struction is the vast amount paid to land-
owners who block the way. In many cases,
this item forms much more than half the
cost, and it is safe to assert that three-
fourths of this cost, together with much
legal expense involved in arriving at a
settlement, are fraud pure and simple. As
a general rule, it may be assumed that the
State pays five times the market value for
every inch of soil resumed, and while the
present system lasts, and while “expert”
and “ Ananias ” remain convertible terms,
and juries and arbitrators maintain their
present high-water mark of imbecilityand cor-
ruption, this evil is incurable. The simple
remedy, as The Bulletin has often pointed
out, lies in an enactment providing that
State or Local Government land-tax valua-
tions—with, perhaps, 10 percent, added as
compensation for disturbance—shall be the
price at which all future resumptions shall
be made, and that no Ministry shall have
power to alter the valuation in any way, or
under any circumstances. Such an enact-
ment would at once reduce the cost of rail-
way construction by probably 40 per cent.
And as the natural corollary of this statute a
Betterment Law should be enacted so that
the cost of a new railway would be borne in
part by those adjacent proprietors whose
land-values are enhanced by the public
expenditure. If the State levied from these
individuals even 30 per cent, of the addi-
tional value conferred upon their properties
by the construction of railways or other
public works, it would effect a great saving,
and a further revenue would be derived from
the land-tax on the enhanced value of the
properties. By these devices it may
reasonably be assumed that the cost of
railway construction could be reduced
by 70 per cent. —in some cases by an
even larger proportion. Litigation could
be obviated, under the Betterment Act, as
under the Land Resumption Act, by the
simple and equitable principle of making
each landlord assess the added value of his
own property, and —where the estimate was
grossly inadequate and no reasonable settle-
ment could be arrived at—giving the State
authority to take over the property at the
owner’s valuation and sell it for its own
profit. By this strictly just policy the cost
of railway construction, in any ordinary
country, could probably be brought down
to not over £3OOO per mile, instead of the
£13,000 which has been the average rate for
New South Wales and Victoria in the past.
# • «
Even at this figure, however, railways
cannot, at present, be built to any large
extent out of revenue. Also, however favor-
able the money market may be, and however
low the cost of construction, there are many
thousands of reasons why not another mile
of line should be built with money borrowed
at interest either in England or Australia. As
an example of possible ways and means the
case of New South Wales may be taken as
an example. In that province the actual
bank-note circulation stands at £1,500,000,
with a slight tendency to increase in propor-
tion to the increase of population. It re-
mains so steadily at, or about, this figure,
that £1,500,000 may be taken as the amount
of paper-circulation at present required to
carry on the business of the community. If
the State took over the entire business of
issuing notes it may therefore be assumed
that there would be no difficulty in keeping
£1,500,000 worth of legal tender paper in
circulation. If the banks doing business in
the province were required to keep a small
portion of their local reserves in the same
State-guaranteed currency, the issue could
easily be raised to £3,000,000. This
amount should be ample for the require
ments of many years to come, therefore the
issue could be absolutely limited to this
figure ; and, with so small an amount in cir-
culation, depreciation, under any reasonably
solvent Government, would be impossible.
* # *
Under an efficient betterment law and an
equitable land resumption system, the afore-
said £3,000,000 would be sufficient to build,
by careful instalments, say, 1000 miles of
railroad. These lines would not cost the
State a single sovereign in gold. They
would be required to pay nothing save actual
working expenses and a small percentage on
their nominal cost with which to redeem the
State paper issued for their construction,
and when it was fully redeemed the country
would possess 1000 miles of railroad, free of
debt and costing absolutely nothing. Even
the nominal price of construction would be so
small that the lines might easily be made to
pay a dividend of at least two per cent. —an
amount which would be only equal to ten
shillings per cent, on the cost of a similar
line built under the extravagant principles
hitherto in vogue. The Bulletin firmly
believes that, under such careful manage-
ment as that of Commissioner Eddy, it is
possible, even in these times of depression,
to build, within the next few years, 1000
miles of railway, either in New South Wales
or Victoria, which would not only pay
working expenses but would also pay
a dividend on the original outlay, if
constructed on the system above described.
And it maintains that this scheme is the only
one yet formulated which will permit of rail-
way extension oq any adequate scale under
present conditions. The old conditions were
suited well enough to the lavish times when
Australia was rich, and took no thought for
the morrow, and the curse of foreign capital
had not yet come home to roost. Now,
however, that the country is poor, and the
morrow which seemed so far off has arrived,
it is necessary to start on an entirely new
plan. The Bulletin is not necessarily
wedded to its own particular scheme ; if a
better can be suggested it will be glad to
advocate it to the best of its ability. But,
until a better is suggested, it presents this
plan as one which, in honest and capable
hands, will fill a dire emergency.
• * 9
The great difficulty in the way of its
adoption is the unreasoning dread, enter-
tained by the half-educated politicians of
Australia, of anything bearing even the sem-
blance of a forced paper currency. The great
majority of these individuals have no reason
for the faith that is in them save a vague
knowledge that, in many cases, such a cur-
rency has ended in serious depreciation and
consequent embarrassment. Not one person
in a thousand studies the causes which led
to this depreciation ; the great majority only
know that it took place, and assume,
because it took place, it is the necessary
accompaniment of an irredeemable—even
temporarily irredeemable - paper currency
under all circumstances. As a matter of
fact, under certain conditions, all currencies
are liable to depreciate. Silver is just now
at a woeful discount. Gold, at one time, in
Australia, was depreciated to a greater ex-
tent than the paper rouble is now in Russia.
And Government paper money has suffered a
serious fall where it has been issued in enor-
mously larger quantities than the community
required, and by States whose credit is at a
very low ebb. In Russia, for instance,
where the paper circulation is nearly
£200,000,000, Italy (£140,000,000), Argen-
tina, Paraguay, and other countries, where
the circulation is fully eight times the
actual requirement, and where there is no
earthly prospect of redemption, paper is con-
siderably below par, and is likely to remain
there. It suffers as any other article does
when sold in large quantities by forced
auction on a falling market. But there is no
case on record where paper money issued by
a solvent constitutional Government which
was not in danger from internal or
external war, the said paper being
issued in just sufficient quantities to
answer ordinary commercial purposes,
and used for reproductive public works
only, has undergone depreciation. There
have been few instances, certainly, in
which a non-metallic currency was issued on
an honest and scientific basis, but in those
few instances it has invariably retained its
value. In any case, however, even allowing
for all risks, real and imaginary, the experi-
ment is worth trying. No risk involved
could be greater than that implied in a con-
tinuance of the borrowing policy which the
old pioneers inaugurated so gaily.
The Meaning of Muckneillism.
Editor Bulletin,— My corpse, when I have
one, will wear on its forehead a small hole made
by the ring of a man whose fight I tried to stop.
That was some time ago, when I was younger,
and taught Sunday-school, and believed there
was virtue in the text which blesses peace-
makers. Ever since then, I have doubted the
solidity of certain integral chunks of “ the im-
pregnable rock of Holy Scripture;” and, when-
ever I see a fight, I content myself with execut-
ing strategic movements round the outskirts
and cheering the combatants on. Consequently,
I have waited to make sure that “Tom Col-
lins” and “Titus Salt” have definitely
slaughtered one another, and finally retired
each to his own wigwam to hang up the other’s
reeking scalp, before venturing to rise and
address you on the subject of the orthodox
morality.
It was never absolutely certain what tree
those redoubtable warriors were barking up ;
but, apparently, Tom asserted moral nations
were the bravest, and Titos said no, they
weren’t. Neither troubled to define bravery or
morality ; but, as the fight proceeded, it
beeai ite clear that by bravery they meant
physical bravery, and by morality sexual con-
tinence according to conventional notions;
Now, all the casuists put what they call
“moral courage” higher than physical courage;
though I can’t see it myself. To me it appears
easier, say, to endure for conscience’ sake the
anger of friends, or the frown of the world,
than to stand up and he killed in somebody
else’s quarrel. In the one case you have always
self-approval and the hope of living to triumph
after all; in the other case you’re mutton, and
there’s an end of everything. But let that
pass, and consider “morality.” Undoubtedly,
what is commonly meant by an immoral man is
a man who is familiar with a woman not his
wife, and who is found out. The finding-out
is essential. “ Society ” generally gives a man
the benefit of the doubt, and holds him
innocent until he is proved guilty. If he is
sufficiently high up like the Duke of
York, for example proof is impossible.
Did all the Totties in Montreal, and all the
admirals in the British fleet, come forward to
testify against such an exalted personage, the
Archbishop of Canterbury would still solemnly
assure the GoD-fearing British public that its
future monarch was vinjo Intacta. A duke
cannot under any circumstances be immoral;
and in proportion as a man approaches ducal
rank more proof of immorality is required.
Even Judge Hanganflog would probably not
convict a C.M.G. on the evidence of less than
nine private detectives—or three reputable
witnesses. But if John Smith, laborer, is not
immoral simply because he is a laborer, he
becomes so the moment he takes Mary Jones
down the harbor on Sunday. Any respectable,
jury will henceforth award heavy damages,
against John Smith.
* * #
Of course, the moment the question is looked
into, it is clear that no arbitrary regulations for
the formation of sex-relationships can ever be a
standard of abstract right and w r rong. Morality
used to be founded on religious dogma, com-
prised in various systems of sanctions and,
prohibitions invented by priestcraft and pub-
lished as supernatural. A great portion of
mankind still reveres the barbaric dicta of so-
called divine revelations ; and the whole human ,
race is more or less influenced by them. But
now that science has put out the fires of hell,
modern philosophy has formulated the simple
and rational creed that morality is to be tested i
by utility. All actions which profit the com-,
munity are moral actions : all which hurt it are.
immoral. Yet even here we reach no ultimate*
standard. The conduct which helps a com:
munity at one time may not help it at another
time; conduct which profits one community may,
injure another differently situated. This, it is;
true, does not concern the individual, who has;
only to apply the maxim, “Do as you wouldl
be done by,” wherever he may be. But an un-
easy feeling is growing up that the interest off
the individual frequently conflicts with that off
the community, so far as it is ascertainable..
Philosophy can only find its moral authority in
regarding both interests as identical. For if
the interest of the individual does conflict with
that of the community, the welfare of the mass
is of less importance to the unit than his own
welfare. Why should he sacrifice himself for
others without return ? Religion replies : in
order to get a harp and crown in the next
world. But there is considerable doubt about
the next world, nowadays : the harp is out of
tune, and the crown is rusty. Philosophy can
only beg the question by reiterating that the
sacrifice of each is repaid by the sacrifice of all,
which is just what is disputed. And the
anxious enquirer remains in his hole.
# # •
Take the monogamistic marriage as an illus-
tration. Now, the limitation of one man to
one woman in some societies is no more moral
than the limitation of one man to six women in
others. A man in Turkey, with half-a-dozen
wives, is just as moral as a man in Australia
with a single follower. If anything, the Aus-
tralian is less moral in the abstract, because
monogamy is unnatural, and the average man
satisfies his primitive desire for change in ways
which are decidedly prejudicial to the com-
munity. As a matter of fact, the conditions of
sexual union vary indefinitely, and the artificial
restrictions of custom can under no circum-
stances be accepted as a test of morality.
Love is as natural and as necessary as hunger,
and there is no more essential immorality
about the pairing of any two human beings
by common consent than there was about
the pairing of the mythical Adam and l
Eve in the Garden of Eden. Savage nations i
love as simply as they eat and drink. As
civilisation has become complex, various regu-
lations have been made in the interest, or the
supposed interest, of society ; but their obliga-
tion is purely voluntary. And, if it does not
suit the individual to guide his conduct by
these regulations, philosophy may hold him im-
moral from the point of view of the community;;
but it has no warrant for considering him im-
moral in se. Still less has religion —particularly
that form of religion which, singularly enough;,
condemns illegitimacy and suicide, though itself
founded on illegitimacy and consummated ini
suicide. Illegitimacy, indeed, whatever its;
conventional character, can never bear a moral!
reproach. The mumbling of a particular for-
mula by a man in a black coat has no bearing:
whatever upon the instinct which leads to*
sexual union, or upon its consequence in the
sacred mystery of birth. What is perfectly
natural must always be perfectly moral.
• • #
It is the fashion, of course, to consider
sexuality an unclean thing—a manifestation of
the degenerate flesh which must be crushed out
by the soaring spirit. This conception of a
physiological function, in which the quality
of purity or impurity can no more inhere than
in the sparkle of a dewdrop or the rustle of a
leaf, we owe to the Oriental religions of which
Christianity is the modern exemplar best known
to us. This it was which colored the teaching


of Jesus; which caused the mutilation of
Origen ; which pointed the temptations of St.
Jerome and St. Anthony ; which filled monas-
teries and nunneries with celibates; which
introduced the church service for the purifica-
tion of women after childbirth ; which lies at
the root of Muckneillism, with its horror
of the nude and its detestation of danc-
ing. Yet dancing is essentially a religious
custom, that is to say, a sexual custom—religion
being for the most part merely a manifestation
of omnipotent sex. When, guided by history,
we survey the workings of this mysterious
principle, and note how, to this day, the arrival
at puberty is among savage nations the occasion
of religious rites which survive in the “ con-
firmation services” of Christian churches; how
nearly the whole of religious “conversions”
are effected during adolescence, when sexual
passion is strongest; how epidemics of erethism
invariably coincide with religious revivals, and
issue in the gambols of the Early Christians, in
the orgies of sects like the Russian “ Christs,
or in the practices which have made choir-
meetings and Sunday-school picnics a by-
word; how the religious instinct is strongest
in women, who are especially creatures of
sex; how girls like Bernadette of Lourdes,
on reaching puberty, are markedly subject
to religious hallucinations ; how religious
exaltation like that of Annie Besant in her
youth finds vent in ecstatic self-devotion as
the spouse” or “the bride” of a sensuous
Christ ; how frequently parsons have large
families, and how curates and priests are adored
by the women—particularly single women ; how
countries like France, where religion is in dis-
repute, have a low birth-rate, and countries
like England, where piety is prevalent, have a
high one ; how religion is strongest among the
poorer classes, who propagate rapidly, and
weakest among the rich, who propagate slowly;
how the modern decadence of religious
beliefs has been accompanied by a corres-
ponding decrease of virility—the marriage-age
showing a steady tendency to rise, while, as
Grant Allen puts it, “ young men are less of
young men than formerly ;” how people leave
off going to church when they get married;
how, in countries like India, where religion
still directs the minutest actions of life, child-
marriage is a national custom ; how sexual
criminals, like Deeming or Mrs. Needle, are
invariably religious ; how the Biblical heroes
“ after God’s own heart” were all polygamists:
how, in the Salvation Army, it is found that
male soldiers more easily convert the women,
and female soldiers the men ; —when we con-
sider these facts, and the many similar ones
which readily suggest themselves to every
observer, we see the justification for Ellis s
statement that “ the phenomena of the religious
life generally are to a large extent based on
the sexual life,” or for Nordau’s statement
that “ religious mania almost always co-exists
with disturbances of the sexual system, and is,
like them, a manifestation of a morbid state,
proceeding from the same pathological modifi-
cations in the condition of the brain.”
# • *
Thus the religious instinct is merely the off-
spring of the sexual instinct; and in its crusade
against sex religion is acting the part of a
parricidal CEdirus. Yet the role is the only
one possible to it. Amare et venerari vix
deo conceditur; and if humanity spends its
energy in passion it will not save it for religion.
You cannot eat your cake and have it, and the
inculcation of clerical celibacy is based on a
perfectly just and logical appreciation of the
facts. St. Paul knew' what he was talking
about when he said that unmarried men looked
after the Lord, and married men after their
wives. The vital force of every individual has
absolute physical limitations, and the worship of
God is irreconcilable with the worship of Woman.
No man can serve two masters —or a master
and a mistress. In the early days of religion
this great truth was not recognised, and the
ancient temples were tended by both priests and
prostitutes, who pooled their earnings amicably,
as befitted their common origin in primal
human impulse. The sacred prostitution which
some Eastern nations imposed upon all women
survived to a comparatively late age in the
worship of Mylitta, and with the Kadeshim
attached to Hebrew temples. These allies the
priests specially recognised as “ Deva-Dasis,
or “ God’s women.” But as religion waxed
complex, priestcraft understood that women
were rivals and interlopers in its domain, and
so drove them forth from the temples and fas-
tened upon them an opprobrium for which
there is no rational warrant. That the con-
ventional marriage is frequently legalised
prostitution is indisputable ; and the formality
of a contract makes no difference in the penal-
ties which follow sexual excess.
# • «
But the religious standpoint is perfectly in-
telligible. Every concession to the sexual
instinct takes something away from the religious
instinct; every obol offered at the shrine of
Venus is threepence less in the collection-plate.
Thus it is to the interest of the Muckneillists to
denounce dancing, which in civilised communi-
ties has lost its religious character, and is merely
an incitement or a satisfaction of passion. The
most ignorant evangelist knows instinctively
that Woman is his hereditary foe. Her success
is his failure ; and, feeling the inequality of the
conflict on a natural footing, he insists that she
shall submit her charms to the handicap of
high-necked dresses, so as to give him a reason-
able chance. If the age-long contest, Heaven
v. Earth with the Priest as counsel for
Heaven, the Woman to represent Earth, and
the Natural Man as arbiter—every success of
the priesthood has been marked by insistence
that women should wear more clothes. Ihe
first thing a female convert has to do is to put
on an old pair of the missionary’s trousers.
When every lady is robed from neck to knee,
after the fashion of a New South Wales Legis-
lative Council bathing-suit, the missionary
begins to have hope that the male converts’
tribute of cocoanuts will not fail him. Simi-
larly, the Salvation Army exacts the hideous
poke-bonnet as a guarantee of good faith :
the Muckneillists declare that low-necked
dresses are an abomination in the sight
of the Lord. If they could, they would
cover up the women’s faces altogether, as in
Mohammedan countries, where (no doubt partly
as a consequence) every man obeys the mollah
and attends the mosque. For my own part, I
trust that Earth will continue to prevail, hold-
ing with Maitland that “ man is like a tree—
the deeper he strikes his roots into earth, the
higher he climbs towards heaven.” It gives me
great pleasure to see Woman keeping her end
up, whether in a ball-room or elsewhere, and
bearing her share of the cross which.has so fitly
been adopted as the supreme religious symbol.
—Yours, Brigham Brown, M.D., F.R.C.S.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Same Old Discrepancy.
A land-resumption case which made a fitful
appearance last week in the New South Wales
law-courts is about the strongest proof yet
offered of the need for a taxation system under
which every owner will value his own property,
pay tax on his own valuation, and be “ re-
sumed ” by the State—if the State feels so in-
clined—also on his own valuation. The matter
involved was 12 acres of land alongside the
railway line in the neighborhood of Mittagong.
A valuable quarry is included in the property,
and the railway commissioners proposed to take
it over for ballasting purposes. Their own
experts estimated the 12 acres at £l5OO, and
there is no evidence at present available that
this estimate didn’t express their conscientious
opinion of the value of the property. The
owner’s experts estimated it—no doubt quite as
conscientiously—at £209,000, or a little more
than 139 times the other experts’ valuation.
Then seven of the most expensive New South
Wales lawyers attended to argue the matter
out before a Judge and jury, and to call a
large amount of high-priced evidence, and
to accumulate legal expenses in a manner
dreadful to behold. The results of the
case have no bearing on the question
under discussion. The matter, as it affects
the community at large, is this: So long
as one crowd of experts is prepared, on its
solemn oath, to value a piece of land at £l5OO,
and another crowd is prepared to value the
same piece, on its equally solemn oath, at
£209,000, and the matter has then to be settled
by hiring several thousand pounds’ worth of
lawyers, who have no personal knowledge of
the value, to argue the matter before a Judge,
who is equally ignorant, and the final decision
rests with a jury which, in nine cases out of
ten, knows nothing at all about it, even after
hearing the evidence and arguments, so long
will the resumption of land for public purposes
continue to be a great and horrible fraud on the
State treasury. In the present instance, the
two lots of experts were a little wider apart in
their estimates than usual, but otherwise there
is no difference between this case and any
other. Two crowds of experts are always
equally positive ; the Judge and the jury have
only the testimony of the experts to go upon,
and there is absolutely no sure method of
arriving at the truth, even if there is any
definite truth to arrive at, which is by no means
certain. Also, if the truth should be arrived
at, it costs so much in legal expenses that it is
hardly worth having. Therefore, every poli-
tician who has the welfare of the country at
heart—if there are any such individuals—should
insist on embodying in the Land-tax Bill the
simple and efficient device, now employed in
Maoriland, whereby every land-holder values
his own property, and the State has the right
of resumption on the basis of his own valuation
if it considers his estimate too low, or it it
wants his estate for public purposes. Consider-
ing that the amount involved in the AmOs case,
apart from legal expenses, would probably be
equal to six months’ land-tax revenue for the
whole of New South Wales, on the anticipated
basis of Id. in the £, this proviso is quite as
important as is the land-tax itself.
A Storm Brewing.
The Victorian Assembly has decided in favour
of a tax on land-values, and with a majority of
14 supporting the main principles of his bill
Premier Turner should get it strengthened,
rather than weakened, in committee. There is
no loophole of escape for any bogus land-taxers
who may have voted with the majority in the
flesh, whilst siding with Fat Man in the spirit.
The only amendments possible under the cir-
cumstances will make things more unpleasant
for the typical stout gentleman aforesaid, so
now that the bill must pass he may be expected
to assist the “moderate parly ” in carrying the
original clauses. Those who hate the bill will
vote against nearly all the amendments. And
then ! The last hope of the Tories is expressed
with artless impudence by sundry up-country
publications and specially stupid members of
the Upper ’Ouse. The poor old Argus merely
talks about a general rising of the community
against a land-values tax, as though the com-
munity hadn’t recently risen for it. J. B.
Patterson delivers himself of the sphinxy pro-
phecy that “ it will never become law.” The
fatuous, frock-coated cormorants of Collins-st.
and Flinders Lane encourage the humble members
of their push to go around buzzing “ They’ll
never get it.” If asked to explain why Victoria
cannot move with the times, these wiseacres
smile as they were wont to smile two or three
months ago, when prognosticating a glorious
victory for Freetrade. They are discreetly
vague. But, by such authorities as T. Brunton,
M.L.0., and the Mount Alexander Mail, the
awful truth is bellowed forth. Brunton,
M.L.0., speaking for the Upper House, in-
formed a rural banquet, or a carefully-selected
shire luncheon, or some equally representative
gathering, that the Upper House would either
throw the iniquitous tax out of the Upper
House’s back door “ or lay it aside.” And the
Mount Alexander Mail, quoted by the Argus,
puts the conundrum to its 18 or more constant
readers, “ How can the Turner Government
I, .pe to force the tax on unimproved
1,i,1 I-values through the Council, even by
a tack ? ” There is a wild idea in
the air that the Council can finally reject a
Budget, or, easier still, “ lay it aside ” and go
on snoring as before. Which idea is born of
Tory faith in its Council’s determination to die
fighting the land-tax if necessary. Much yalu-
able time would be lost before the political
destruction of Bbunton, William Clarke, and
the rest of them was satisfactorily accom-
plished, and herein lies the Upper House s hope
of a compromise. The country would have to
wait for financial legislation of urgent import-
ance whilst its Parliamentary system was
getting reconstructed. The Bruntonites, if
they have a majority, will back Time against
the land-tax, and. after compelling the tax to
pay forfeit, they will await the almost certain
punishment of death with a martyr smile on
their capitalistic countenances.
Boodlewraith. Floats a Loan.
As anticipated in a recent Bulletin, Queens-
land Government has sprung a new loan upon
the province in almost the last week of the
Parliamentary session. Only a week before
Premier Nelson told the public that —
I think it advisable at the present moment to avoid
b't rowing more money. In the interests of the colony it
will be wise for a session, at any rate, to abstain from
goi ig to the English public to ask for the loan of further
sums of money.
Eight days passed ; Parliament was within a
week of prorogation ; and Treas. Nelson came
down to the Assembly and demanded authority
for a .£2,000,000 loan. It is explained that
there is a vital difference between borrowing
money for public works —to which the
Treasurer referred in his quoted utterance —
and borrowing money to wipe out Treasury
Bills—-to which he refers at present. In the
one case, it seems, you impair the credit of the
province ; in the other case you strengthen its
credit by showing investors how honestly you
are striving to pay your debts—by floating
another IO U. Thanks to the “ heaven-born
financing” of MTlwbaith, Nelson, and Co.,
Queensland has accumulated (as per the
Auditor-General’s last report) a revenue deficit
of ,£1,674,686. This has been wiped out in the
public ledger by selling land for ,£274,064, and
by borrowing .£1,400,622 on Treasury Bills.
The Govt, now proposes to convert £1,128,400
of bills into inscribed stock, with a 40 years’ cur-
rency —that is, it deliberately refuses to meet its
obligations, and shunts the losses of its bungled
finance on to the shoulders of the Queenslanders
of 1935. A more dishonest proposal was never
submitted to any Australian Parliament. So
far, every Govt, has recognised its responsibility
of making revenue meet expenditure. But the
chronic necessities of Boodlewraith banks have
choked whatever sense of responsibility Q. Govt,
possesses. It sets the precedent of wiping out
deficits by long-dated promissory notes ; and, so
long as the notes are taken up, Boodlewraith’s
troubles about repayment! The Queensland
National Bank, in which many members of
the Govt, party are deeply interested as
directors, or shareholders, or depositors, has
nominally upwards of £3,000,000 of Govt,
money on hand; and if the projected loan is
floated it will have upwards of £5,000,000. In
addition to the Treasury Bills, the new loan
provides £765,000 to take up old stock, and
£106,000 is set apart to meet expected flotation
charges (the loss on the last loan of £1,182,400
was £168,167 !) £1,265,000 of liabilities falls
due on Ist January, 1896—75 months 0ff—
£228,000 on Ist April, 1896—76 months off—
ami £400,000 on Ist July, 1896—79 months off.
Yet Treasurer Nelson considers it imperatively
necessary to float his loan at once and saddle
the province with double interest, from a year
to a year and a-half before the money is publicly
needed !
A Meeting of Victoria’s Creditors.
Victobia called a meeting of her creditors last
week in re an outstanding debt of £73,000; but,
for once in the history of the province, a divi-
dend of one farthing in the pound was not
accepted. This debt is part of Patterson’s
scheme of preferential repudiation, submitted
to the country under the alias Retrenchment.
Patterson, on his own motion, held back
£73,000 due to the public servants, and the
amount is still outstanding, and could be re-
covered by process of law. The Age, pre-
sumably inspired, suggested voluntary abandon-
ment of the debt, and H. N. P. Wollaston,
Secretary for Customs, called a meeting of
public servants for the said purpose. Mr.
Wollaston took the horse to the water, and it
kicked him in. Under the guidance of a young
man eloquent, one Harry Dagleish, the meet-
ing spurned Mr. Wollaston, icfused to con-
sider his proposal, and remitted the question
to Parliament. The Victorian Parliament has
already “ retrenched ” the Service twice,
so Mr. Dagleish probably acted on the idea
that lightning does not strike three times in the
same place. His notion was, however, as much
as possible politic and as little as possible
politics, and so served its purpose. A few days
before this meeting, that fulsome person, Frank
Madden, declared, out of the abundance of his
ignorance, that Victoria was within measurable
distance of asking time to pay her creditors;
and, on the same day, a few hours previous,
Premier Turner, speaking with knowledge,
announced Victoria’s two alternatives a
Land - tax or Repudiation. There is no
dilemma here. The Victorian people are
ready to adopt the Land : tax, and abandon
all peddling schemes for filching pence from the
pockets of the public servants. The Land-tax
is not a blow at anything but monopoly, which
takes all and gives nothing—while the public
servants give at least some return for their
salaries.

Fading Away.
The acceptance by Premier Turner (Vic.) of
the challenging motion of a private member-
unrecognised as the leader of any party —as a
vote of want of confidence, inherently taking
precedence of all other Parliamentary business,
is a sign that party government, “ Government
by Contradiction,” is slowly dying of a compli-
cation of diseases. By the precedents of party
government, Turner’s orthodox course would
have been to treat M‘Kenzie’s motion with
scorn, by classing what purported to be a
mortal challenge as an ordinary motion and
leaving it to take its chances on the routine
business paper, without swerving a hair’s-
breadth from the announced Government pro-
gramme. Precedents die hard usually. Wheu
they are departed from, almost as a matter ol
course, as in the case under consideration, the
interpretation of the sign—generally ignored
because it is an ordinary fact under
everybody’s vision —is that the systen
of which the precedents formed a part it
breaking towards dissolution. The acceptancd
of M'Kenzie’s motion, as if M‘Kenzie were the
recognised leader of the Victorian Opposition,
is tantamount to a denial of Patterson, the
acknowledged chief of that Opposition. Hie
denial of the chief means the denial of the
party. The denial of the party involves, in the
course of future progression, the denial of the
system of “ Government by Contradiction.
Evolutionary prophecy already sees the foetus
taking shape in the womb of the future—a
Premier and Minister balloted for separ-
ately and for set terms (with a proviso
for expulsion under certain circumstances), and
any Parliamentary nobody challenging ad-
ministrative proposals without necessarily
endangering the tenure of the adminis-
tration, or even of any individual administrator.
In those days, the grave of the monster Govern-
ment by Coalition will be five hundred fathoms
deep.
Politics and Perjury.
The squalid meanness of Party Government
was never better demonstrated than by the
stand which the Dibbs party is making against
the New South Wales Land and Income-tax
Bill. That same party, when it was in power,
tried to etiforce similar taxes, and the present
Reid crowd, or as much of it as was then in
Parliament, mostly viewed the proposition with
horror. Now the Reid crowd is running the
scheme, and the Dibbs crowd—which, if it had
any brains at all, would simply congratulate
its enemies on their conversion, and shove the
bill along, on the ground that it is its own bill
—takes its turn at regarding the scheme with
horror. Neither party, in the main, is sincere,
and the party which is lying on one side of the
question would lie quite as effusively on the
other if the occasion demanded. In fact, each
party has lied effusively on both sides of the
question already, and has an unlimited capacity
for keeping on doing so. And the country is
so used to this blatant selfishness that it
isn’t at all impressed. Party Govern-
ment admittedly exists for the purpose
of shoving the party into office, therefore it is
a recognised thing that the Ministerial party
should support the Ministerial proposals what-
ever they may be, and that the Opposition
should oppose them, whatever they may be.
And when, through a not infrequent turn of
circumstances, one gang is required to cheer
wildly for the scheme which it regarded (when
in Opposition) with disgust, and the other one
is required to utterly loathe the infamous pro-
posals which (when it wasn’t in Opposition) it _
enthusiastically advocated, the public is so
accustomed to the situation that it entirely
fails to see the naked infamy of it.
In the Victorian no-confidence debate last week,
Wheeler, M.P., rose when the clock said past
midnight to oppose the taxon unimproved land-
values. That very afternoon he accompanied
a deputation to urge a tax on the unearned
increment on sheep :
Mr. Wheeler ask-d that the Govt, should introduce a
bill authorising the formation of a trust which would be
empowered to levy a tax of 2s. for every 100 sheep in the
province, for the purpose of getting funds to carry on the
export trade in frozen mutton Unless this
was done some of the owners of sheep who would derive a
benefit by the export of mutton relieving a glutted market
and so sending up the price of sheep would not contribute
anything towards the enterprise, and it was to avoid
them getting an unfair advantage that this course was
suggested. It was felt that all those who would obtain a
benefit from the export trade should contribute towards
the establishment of that trade. As an instance of what
the development of this trade had done so far.it was
stated that since mutton had been exported from here
during the lust few months longwool merinoes had ad-
vanced Is. per head, and this advantage was being derived
mostly by owners of large Hocks of sheep who had not
assisted the export industry in any way.
With almost trifling changes this speech would
do for advocacy of the land-tax. Wheeler’s
opposition to the land-tax iu the early morning
can therefore be pub down to sleepiness.
One and all of the approvers of the drastic
(seven-year) sentences passed by N.S.W. Chief
Justice Darlev on the Momba and Kallara
shearers, base their judgment on the principle
that the crimes imputed to the accused were as
heinous committed when and where they were
as if committed at any time anywhere. The
Court stated this principle in exact terms, and
the daily Press, taking up the words, hammered
out an apparently reasonable deduction. But,
first, should any human act, virtuous or crimi-
nal, be, in estimating its true value, dissociated
from its surroundings? Momba and Kal-
lara were in the throes of an aggravated
trade-strike, and partisans on both sides
were charged with feelings which goaded
to violence. This does not excuse the
perpetrators of outrage, but it should be mani-
fest that it enters into every dispassionate
attempt to calculate the actual guilt of the
accused, and into every sane attempt to appor-
tion adequate punishment. In the next place,
does not this chorus of approval rise from the
very crowd of persons who argue day after day
that certain bank swindlers should be released
from gaol, on the ground that their offences
against the law were perpetrated at a time of
abnormal financial excitement ? If it is right to
regard the crimes of those financiers through
the atmosphere of the time, whatever difference
may exist as to the amount of the influence
thus exercised ; why should the disturbing in-
fluence of a bitter strike be expressly excluded
when offending shearers are the theme ?

SUNDRY SHOWS.

Priecksss Theatre Comic Opera Co. Farewells.
Theatre Royal Masfgie Moore Co.
Bijou ... “ Morocco Bound."
“ The Streets of London,” which spreads
its lachrymose dimensions over the stage at
Sydney Royal, may almost claim the honor of
being the most weepful drama of the present day.
It is full of tears and lamentations and damp with
unutterable grief. The heroine lies on a thorny
bed of care all through four desolate acts, and
gaunt hunger sits on her threshold, and woe gib-
bers at her in her dreams and in her awakening.
The beginning of the story is obviously an adapta-
tion of Charles Reade’s “Hard Cash,” an air of
originality being lent to the new version by the
wicked banker scooping £30,000 instead of £14,000,
and the captain dying in the presence of Badger
instead of going mad in the presence of Skinner.
From this point, however, it roams away into
paths of its own, and all resemblance to anything
is lost. All the really good people get out on the
streets and wander round in black, weeping
bitterly, with their hair down, and looking vainly
for a crust. They lose their billets and never find
any other ones, and as the natural consequences
they go for unprecedented periods without food,
and think bitter thoughts about mother. The
heroine, dressed in black as aforementioned,
becomes almost too feeble to articulate, and
can hardly lie down without holding on
with both hands, and the prevailing impres-
sion is that she hasn’t had any dinner for
eleven weeks. Her brother reposes at full length
in the street for 45 minutes from sheer exhaus-
tion. The banker’s accomplice sells matches fora
living, and the heroine’s lover pawns his overcoat
and lives on baked-potatoes. Even the baked-
potato-man is sold up three times by the same
wicked banker. And all this while the accom-
plice, Badger, has a document in his pocket with
which he can bring the banker to the dust at any
moment, but every time, when he is just on the
point of getting there, something unexpected hap-
pens to overthrow Badger and postpone his ven-
geance. And when, at last, the document is
produced, all the hungry victims who have been
weeping in the streets for years past rise up and
forgive the banker, after all, for the utterly irrele-
vant reason that he “loves his daughter,” The
most picturesque characters in the east are Alfred
Woods, as Badger ; Miss Vivienne, as the princi-
pal weepist in the streets; and Miss Austin Eliot,
as the banker’s hard-faced daughter. These people
all do remarkably good work, and the others
do nothing in particular, and do it in no
particular fashion. A widow and a duke, who
appear on the bill, don’t transpire in the flesh at
all ; and a well-staged conflagration is somewhat
marred by the fact that the lachrymose people,
who are living in a cottage out in the country, see
the blaze, in the very far distance, and at once
recognise that it is at 219 J Cross-street, White-
chapel, or something of the sort, that the banker
is the incendiary, and that he is trying to burn
the document. This awful certainty about the
odd half in the street number is dispiriting.
** * *
•“ Ransom,” G. Rignold’s latest effort,
holds an honorable position among melodramas,
inasmuch as it is less confused than the ordinary
melodrama, and doesn’t outrage time and space,
and the haggard head of anachronism doesn’t
glare too conspicuously among the flowers. It is
a romance of an ideal selector, in the person of
Scot Inglis, with Miss Athena Claudius for his
ideal wife, living in an ideal homestead in the
bush. There are no unkempt children about that
homestead, no goats, no pigs rooting in the
garden, and no hens climbing on the kitchen-
table, nor has the family horse come in and rubbed
his tongue over the salt-cellar, and the whole hired
staff consists of bright little Dora Mostyn, as an
ideal boy. To prove that he is an ideal boy, this
boy is made to deliver an impassioned address
concerning the Melb. Cup. But the shadow of
the wicked squattah is over the ideal homestead.
The squattah wants Miss Claudius to be the
flower of his harem, and he arranges the cap-
ture of her husband by bushrangers. These
outlaws demand a ransom of .£3OO, or else
they will hang their captive’s handsome head
by a strong string upon a tree, and the
squattah puts up the money on condition
that Min* Claudius will be his’n in return. Her
husband being saved, that lady goes forth weeping
to deliver herself up to the squatbah as a burnt-
offering in a gully, but the ideal boy and the
selector get there before her and bang her enemy
considerably against a tree. The selector also
comes into a fortune and pays off his debt to the
immoral pastoralist, and as he is going out after
getting his receipt a bushranger comes in and
shoots the man of wool and beeves. Naturally
that vast and boundless ass, the law, interferes
just here and sends the ideal selector to Berrima
for life, but things are cleared up later on, and he
is left embracing his wife on his own freehold
property.
• • •
Outside this story there runs another
wholly irrelevant tale about a stout lady who has
come to Australia to reduce her fat, her trodden-
on husband, her daughter who won’t “ hold her-
self up,” and a new-chum. The new-chum is
hereby consigned to oblivion, but the stout lady,
in the capable hands of Miss Kate Bishop, is the
best character in the drama. Dora Mostyn’s ideal
boy is also a refreshing oasis; Dora is bright and
pretty and decidedly impressive in her pants and
tall boots, and she sings rather nicely. Scot
Inglis—who doesn’t sing, by the way—is a parti-
cularly fine selector ; and—with the exception of
the new-chum aforesaid, one or two of the bush-
rangers, and a long, stork-like personage in the
medical profession everybody fits his part
admirably. The sight of Miss Claudius arriving
on horseback with the ransom is in itself worth
threepence of the admission money, if not more.

At Sydney Lyceum, the “ Foundling” has
been gradually remaining about the same as
before. There is so little to be ri ,ne with the
unpromising materials on hand that, this result is
only what might have been expected. Opinions
differ a good deal as to whether the co. or the
comedy is most at fault. The Bulletin's opinion
is that they are eminently worthy of each other;
the comedy is a dreary and confused affair, and half
theco. could well besent back again to England with
advantage, and its place filled with local talent.
One gladsome piece of news is that the “Found-
ling” will retire this week to make room for
“Jones,” which goes up on Saturday. “ Jones ”
is by Arthur Shirley, the author of sundry gore-
draggled dramas, but it is alleged to be a bright
farcical-comedy none the less. At all events its
name is a recommendation, although Shirley
possibly selected it because it rhymed so pleasantly
with “ bones ” and “groans.”
* *- * *
Edison’s kinetoscope (on view at 148 Pitt-
street, Sydney) is the newest entertainment at
present in Sydney. Outwardly, the kinetoscope
looks like a mildly-glorified peep show, but it
differs from the peep-show in exactly the same
way a runaway horse differs from a sausage.
The figures keep moving all the time, and move
as naturally as they do in real life. The pride
and glory of the present collection is the skirt-
dancer, who is probably the best of her kind
yet seen in Australia. Her high-kicking is a
thing of beauty; and her bright~smile, and her
waving draperies, and the airy boundings of her
two small feet, make the spectator long to burst
into the instrument and embrace this vision Sin.
long. The other pictures cover a variety of sub-
jects. A free and independent cock-fight, in
which the smaller rooster wins, is another
vigorous spectacle; but the most desperate
shindy in the collection is a wrestling match,
in which one combatant is thrown six
times in as many seconds, and is several
times lifted up and thrown right over his
opponent’s head. There are unlimited possibilities
in the kinetoscope. It ought to be owned by an
immensely wealthy syndicate, which could send
agents all over the world to photograph prize-
fights, duels, Chinese executions (especially by the
process of cutting into ten thousand slices), fires,
murders, hangings, sinking ships, railway ac-
cidents, Nautch dances, corroborees, coronations,
battles, riots, proposals of marriage, and all
manner of interesting subjects. When this is
done the instrument can be made to show the
history of the world, and anyone can drop in and
see whatever has been happening lately. An
execution, for example, is heavy reading in five
columns of the boy-reporter’s eloquence, but the
kinetoscope could show the whole thing, from the
entrance of the criminal to the inquest, in five
minutes, and not leave out a single detail.

Maggie Moore’s season at Melb. Royal
appears to be going as strong as ever, if not
more so. It is a large house to fill during these
drowsy weeks of waiting for Xmas to come, but
the masses roll up and fill it every night. On
Saturday, “The Child of the Regiment” was
produced for the first time in Melbourne by the
present company, and Maggie Moore’s imper-
sonation of that dashing militairess excited a
£ood deal of wonder as to why she hadn’t done
it before on the same boards. “The Child of
the Regiment” is a joyously impossible yarn
under any circumstances. M. M. makes the
most of the joy and the least of the impos-
sibility. Also, she manages the operatic music
of the part with much ingenuity, and the result
of her exertions is a general opinion that the
curtain falls too soon. On Wednesday, a fare-
well revival of “The Colleen Bawn ” was to
start. Other revivals will follow at brief inter-
vals, until the management get to “Struck Oil,”
after which—the pantomime.

Mrs. Annie Besant gave her last lecture in
Adelaide Town Hall on Tuesday evening, and
next day sailed per mail for India. In all the
considerable cities of Australia and Maoriland she
attracted large audiences ; and, except in Victoria,
she did not visit any up-country town. Geelong,
of course, held aloof from a speaker who reproached
a Philistine clergy with their lack of spirituality ;
but many of the best men of Ballarat went with
their wives to hear the most eloquent of living
women. Outside her oratorical gifts, Mrs. Besant
is a remarkable woman and a great worker. The
nightly lecture was only a small part of her daily
task. From 11 to 2 every day she used to meet
inquirers in public, and later in the afternoon she
gave interviews to occultists. What leisure she
had left was devoted to letter-writing, her corre-
spondents living in all parts of the globe. She
got on very well with R. S. Smythe and his son,
doubtless feeling that, but for their management,
she would have addressed hundreds instead of
thousands.
At Poverty Point.
A star actress, who lately visited Australia, raised Cain
one evening over a performance of “Camille.” In the
last act, when Camille bids farewell to Armand, she pre-
sents him with her photo., which she states is said to be
very like her. Being “realistic,” this particular star
would insist upon the presence of the real article, when-
ever possible, even at rehearsal, and several times
remarked, “ Now, Mr. So-and-so, this is my photo.
Imagine that you are speaking to and looking at me.”
On toe evening in question the property-man gave
Camille the photo., as he thought, of herself, but, when
her ladyship presented the pasteboard, Armand broke
out into a broad grin, which quite spoilt the scene. The
photo, was one of “Japanese Tommy.”
A young and popular actress, whose Galatea is
st:ll fresh in Australian public memory, had the
prettiest scene in the piece once spoilt for her in a
very peculiar way. Just after completing the setting of
the scene the property-man could not, for the life of him
discover the stuffed kid that did duty for the fawn.
Something had to be done, and remembering that some
people living near the theatre were the possessors of a
very .young kid he borrowed it, and rushing with it to a
neighbouring chemist had the animal chloroformed;
quickly returning to the theatre he gave it from the
wings to Lucippe, who afterwards surrendered it to
Galatea. At the moment of Galatea’s w eeping over the
dead fawn, the kid, recovering from the chloroform,
raised its head and, opening its eyes in bleary style,
baa'd out in a decidedly drunken fashion. With the
stubbornness of its kind the animal would persist in giving
expression to its views of the proceedings, and amidst
roars from the audience Pygmalion's best girl abruptly
ended the scene.
Miss Marcella Day, of Melbourne, has unearthed a new
danger to newspaper proprietors by suing the Aob and
Arous for damages on account of an alleged libel in a
certain advt. Miss Marcella appeared at Melb. Alexandra
one Saturday night under Inigo Tyrrell's ill-fated manage-
ment, and sang three songs in a, it is said, more or less pain-
ful manner. The burden of one ditty (from “ Madame
Favart ") was “She is such an artless thing.” Tyrrell’s
business advt. on Monday morning included these words:
" Miss Day will also take ‘ a night off.’ She is such an

The critics, remarks The Bulletin’s London corres-
pondent, have been very kind to Alfred Dumpier and
“ Robbery Under Arms,” just produced at the Princess’s.
Alfred put all he knew into the leading character, whilst
Herbert Flemming was a manly and vigorous Dick
Mars to n. Charles Achurch Charrington got special leave
from Janet to play a desperado, which he did to the
satisfaction of the gallery. He gave full vent to his
voice, and stormed and raved after the most approved
maimer of stage bushrangers. Anna Face-lotion Ruppert
gave a charming interpretation of Starlight’s devoted
Aileen, and what gave even more pleasure than her act-
ing was her appearance on a milk-white palfrey which she
bestrode with much grace. Clarence Holt got a rare
reception, and showed how melodrama should he played
—no mealy-mouthed business, sir, but your real old
Astley’s form. “Robbery Under Arms” should draw
money to pit and gallery, but it will not be a lasting
success. However, Dampier made a good entry on the
London boards, and that is something.
“There is some talk of the formation of a ‘powerful
syndicate ’ to undertake the business of carting out first-
class companies to Australia to play the latest London
successes, in the best style, for a season in Melbourne and
Sydney only. It is hoped that—with the exception of,
perhaps, the actor-manager himself—the original cast will
he secured and special scenery and accessories provided.
The names of some well-known dramatists are mentioned
in connection with the proposal, for there is considerable
dissatisfaction as to the way plays are being run in this
country. Pinero is said to be much displeased with the
financial result of ‘The Second Mrs. Tanqueray.’ ’’
The Pall Mall Gazette man jumps on Captain Star-
light, and jibes at the “Simple people like the Austra-
lians,” who are alleged (by the management) to think
“Robbery Under Arms” a good melodrama. It was
advertised as a phenomenally successful show in its own
country, and as such it is abused by the cynical cockney,
who “likes to know the taste of Australians in these
matters.” In short, the P.M.G. revels in it* dire ignorance
of the public that is catered for by Williamson and Mus-
grove and Brough and Boucioaulfc. A Sydney or Mel-
bourne newspaper might, with as much reason, judge
British taste by one of D in Barry's “ latest London sensa-
tions,” but. we never find a simple Australian journalist
making quite such an ass of himself. Mr. Dumpier, says
P.M.’G., “ lacked vigor and sauntered through his part, as
if it bored him.’* That's fair comment, unhappily. The
actor suffers from a physical ailment which 'makes him
saunter, hut this slow method seems generally acceptable.
As long as he doesn’t rush through his parts and bore
other people, like Lonnen, all is well.
London D.T. deals gently with Alfred Dampier and his
stage version of “ Robbery Under Arms,” at the local
Princess’. “To some the play may be a little noisy, to
others too diffuse, hut on the whole it is healthy, sound,
and m inly, and, if it happens to please a crowded cheap
audience, who would willingly deprive such playgoers of
an innocent evening’s amusement?” Dampier gets praise
for his pleasing voice and “manner of n cultured gentle-
man,” and is said to remind the critic of Henry Neville—
which is about the nicest compliment that can be honestly
paid him, as it happens. There it a something of
Neville in Dampier, although the old London actor uses
his musical voice to more purpose with less effort.

The “Grace Waltz,” composed by Sydney
Turnbull, of Brisbane, and dedicated to Lady
Norman, has come to hand this week. It has
been well received up North, and a number of
musical authorities speak of it in terms of high
commendation.
The Bulletin Christmas Number, to be
published Dec. 15, will be original from cover to
cover ; the track of the kangaroo will adorn every
page on which the emu hasn’t already built a nest;
and all the old Bulletin favorites have combined
with picture, song, and story, to cram as much,
miscellaneous joy into it as 44 pages will hold..

The following paragraph appeared in lasfr
issue of The Bulletin :
A Dubho boy, at boarding-school in Sydney, disliking
the treatment accorded him by the head-master, stole out
of the dormitory one night, with eight shillings in his
pocket, faced the 300 miles that lay before him, and
trudged home in five weeks.
We are informed that the head-master regards the
paragraph as conveying that the pupil was forced
away by ill-treatment. As The Bulletin had
never the slightest intention of conveying any
such insinuation it tenders its apology to the head-
master if, in his opinion, any such meaning is
capable of being attached to the paragraph.
The defeat of Mr. John Hughes, for
Fitzroy Ward, Sydney, last Saturday, by Mr, F,
Penny, means that the somewhat inconsequential I
Mr. Sammy Lees is to be the next Mayor of;
Sydney. Mr. Hughes’ defeat is chiefly due toj
three causes : that he refused to pledge himself to
vote for making Mr. Lees mayor; that he had in-
curred the great displeasure of the big landowners
and contractors by getting the second lot of
Market-site excavations done by day-work instead
of by contract (his action in this regard having
been the means of many hundreds of pounds going
into the pockets of the men instead of into those
of the contractors, who had paid but 6s. a day
wages instead of 7s. paid by the Council—which
nevertheless made a very large saving on the cost
of the work); and that Mr. Penny, being a
licensing-magistrate, was accorded a large section
of the whisky-mill support.
About the first thing which man learned to
do when he emerged from utter barbarism was to
wear some kind of a hat, and he has been growing
more and more particular about his hat ever since.
The primitive Aryan man wore a rag tied round
his head ; his remote descendant of the present
day goes to Smith and Son’s, of 302 George-street,
Sydney, and invests in one of the new Japanese
straws—the lightest, newest and most elegant
kind of skull-gear in the market. Or he can take
his choice among the tennis hats, boating hats,
Terai hats, soft hats, hard hats, cork hats, bell-
toppers, and innumerable other varieties. Every
kind of hat that was ever seen by the eyes of man,
or heard of with his ears, or dreamt of in the
visions of his head upon his bed, can be obtained
from Messrs. Smith and Son at the lowest price ;
together with gold and silver mounted umbrellas,
all other kinds of umbrellas, caps, walking-sticks,
puggaries, and the like frivolities.

The Wild-Cat Column.
It is whispered—and denied by a manufacturer
that the buying of Australian-grown tohacoo-leaf isin the:
hands of a ring. That is why, it is said, tobacco-growing
is not profitable, and why the local article is only
smoked on compulsion. But even if not so, what can be
expected when a manufacturer admits that the only good
tobacco district in Victoria is worked solely by Chinese
tenants? Tenants of whom? The answer would possibly
solve the ring question.
♦ * • •
The Vie. Tariff Commission should, before it
ceases, ascertain whether any Victorian tobacco is sold as
American ; whether any American firm has branches here
and manufactures from Australian leaf, and whether it
put its American trade-mark on tobacco made here from
American or Australian leaf; why it is almost impossible
to get good plugs of a once good black tobacco ; whether
sunflower leaves or other such substiutes are used for
tobacco-leaf ; and whether good and bad (there are no
middlings, the makers admit, in the “colonial” trade)
pieces are ever pressed together intone plug.
Some very peculiar evolutions took place in
connection with a recent big sale of ironmongery stock.
No. I’s offer was shown to No. 2, who rose on it; No. 2’s
was shown to No. 1, who also rose; No. I’s second was
shown to No. 2, who also rose, and was accepted, without
No. 1 being given a show of straddling.
• * * •
The Maoriland Government is issuing locally
per cent, consols at par. If this issue is a success in
Maoriland, which has less available coin than either Vic-
toria or New South Wales, it should put a definite end to
the practice of borrowing on the London market, even for
redemption purposes.
• » ♦ *
A heavy drop has taken place in A.J.S.
Bank shares—which are now offered at 31s. with no busi-
ness doing, since the N.S.W. Land-tax began to be a near
possibility. The A.J.S. is, rightly or wrongly, said to have
liens over something like 300 station properties, and with
this enormous anfount of earth on hand, the Land-tax,
when it comes, will strike it rather heavily.
• • * •
Walter Madden’s Trustees’ and Executors’ Co.
(The National) pays a div. of 10 per cent. Walter puts
down the success to pessimistic valuers who were bilious
even in boom-time. This should for ever settle the
question of Pessimism v. Optimism—for if pessimism will
pay a 10 per cent. div. in the Melb. state of affairs, then
optimism must he at an eternal discount. But, according
to Walter Madden’s political position, it is only civil-
servants who earn any money at all these times. How-
ever, we never expect the private Fat Man to live up to
his political creed.
r » • * *
Farnham Park, Warrnambool (Vic,), was sold
last week to prove that land has no value in Victoria.
The first bid for the first lot was £2O an acre, and the suc-
cessful bid was £35 15s. an acre ; 100 acres went at £2O to
£25 ; 335 acres at £25 to £3O ; 136 acres at about £35 ; 5
acres at £7l. And yet there is no unearned increment to
be taxed, and no means of paying the tax, and no way to
estimate it! The upset price of the first lot was £26, or
£9 15s. under the realised price. The principal pur-
chasers were the “ .oor farmers ” of the district, against
whom Melbourr ,‘nd Maoriland buyers could not con-
tend.

» • • •
There are already six methods of going bung in
Victoria without the publicity of bankruptcy. Still
another plan is now adopted, but so far by the rag
trade only. A private meeting of the creditors agrees to
secretly “write off,” say, 10s. in £, and allow the trader
to carry on as though nothing had happened. Supporters
who are not “ in the know” continue to give long terms
to the trader, whilst those who were "in” before take
care to deal for cash in 14 days only. When the trader
again goes bung, in the old-fashioned way, the outsiders
are “ in ” and insiders are “ out,” after making sufficient
profit between the two busts to compensate for the 10s.
written off.
• • •
Ugly rumours are afloat up North respecting a
well-known Australian firm in the trade-liquidation line.
It is possible the matter may not become public, as more
than one big mercantile firm has been privy to the
swindle, which consists of the assignee invariably doubling
and trebling the amount of certain confederate traders’
legitimate claims on certain estates assigned to the firm
for realisation. Outsiders' claims are, of course, put in at
the right amount and their dividends are diminished,
whilst the conspiring merchant and assignee whack up
100 to 200 per cent, more in the shape of dividend than
they are really entitled to.
• • • •
Several of the erstwhile gilded youths of Bris-
bane, whose fori lines melted away after the boom, have
done well at cane-growing on the Johnstone river. They
have cleared the cost of their land and the initial expenses,
and next season should make a few hundreds each. Cane-
growing promises to eclipse squatting as an industry tor
young men with a little capital and a lot of energy.
♦ * • •
An order for 200 shares at ss. is given to a
’Change broker to be executed next day; next morning
he buys at 4s. 9d., is at once a seller at a profit, which he
pockets, and is once more a buyer, and repeats the opera-
tion as often as possible. If he fills the order it is at full
price to the client at the finish. This is constantly done.
Yet investors express surprise that whether they buy or
sell they almost invariably find they do so at the extreme
market price !
• • • •
A nut for the pro-kanaka crowd to crack : The
Central Mill, Mackay, pays the oanegrowers 14s. a ton for
cane and a share in the factory profit amounting to about
4s. 6d. per ton of cane -and this season has made a profit
of about £16,000. Other millowners pay from 9s. to 10s.
per ton for cane and are making enormous profits. Who
can reasonably say that such an industry could not pay
white men’s wages ?

The senior partner of a big firm is an absentee,
and lately paid a short visit to Australia. The general
manager was talking to a departmental manager,
and, horror! the subordinate had his hat on. The man
from Hingiand was informed that the dept, manager had
been intercepted whilst returning from lunch and hung
his hat up when he reached his department; still, this
could not be admitted as any palliation of the olfence,
and the removal of the hat was insisted upon. The sub-
manager replied by writing out his resignation and so
ending 17 years’ honorable service. The “ English ” style
is always adopted by the Jews, as witness their aggressive
attire at races, regattas, and other public gatherings.
