F.: “Friend ‘M‘G.’ seems very anxious for
new shillings. He says we don’t see new silver nowadays.
As a matter of fact the new designs in British silver coin
are not circulating in Vic. yet, but pathless the cur-
rency is all right. Anyhow, supposing ‘ M‘G.’ gets ss. a
week, he should only see one 1894 coin in a month,
according to the arithmetic of the thing. A shilling
theoretically lasts about 20 years, so that the coins issued
before ’74 should now, for the most part, he out of circu-
lation. But hoarded and lost coins crop up every now
and again to interfere with the rule. I examine seven
coins left in my pocket by Fate. The dates are ’79, ’79,
'79, ’77, ’72, ’B6, 'BB. Are these new enough for * M‘G. ?’
The tendency of the banks is to keep the best silver in
their strongholds. They over-purchased silver in the
boom and now have a mass of it idle ; as a consequence,
silver with current dates is not imported.
“ Regarding old sovereigns in circulation.
Before my last remarks I scanned a draft of 47 sovereigns
got in the ordinary way of business at the National Bank,
Melb. The lot oontaii ed one old Sydney sovereign (straw-
colored because of its silver contents) dated 1856. There
were no coins of the sixties. There were four of the
seventies, and ten of the eighties. ’9O to '92 contributed
10 coins, and there were eight dated ’93, and 14 dated '94.
We may call the ’93 and ’94 coins brand-new, and their
percentage would be 50 per cent, (about) of the whole.”
* • • •
The dirtiness of Australian bank-notes is un-
speakable. To a keen nose it is a concert of perfumes to
smell a hundred consecutive notes. Kerosene, sewerage,
fish, &e. Ugh ! During the influenza boom, three or four
years ago, every note-department in the Melbourne banks
had to give sick-leave to its officers. Germs swarm on
every dirty note, but the banks conceal them in a bundle
with a clean note outside, and the germs get another
chance with the beastly public. When, oh when, is the
State Bank coming along with clean notes?
* • • •
The wholesale jewellers in Melbourne, when a
retailer “ goes,” take an assignment to one of themselves,
and divide the stock among them, thus keeping the
ex.’sdownto a few pounds. Their experience of trade
assignees has taught them that if an estate realised 30s.
in the £, a div. of at the outside 10s. might possibly
reach the creditors.
• * • •
Dear Bulletin, —Your par. re Mdb. Wool-
brokers’ Association is slightly incorrect. Facts as fol-
low ;—The woolbuyers have lately decided not to attend
any sales not conducted in the Wool Exchange ; as in the
bustle of the wool season they lose too much time travel-
ling between the various rooms. The Wool Exchange is
leased by the Woolbrokers’ Association, consequently they
have the power to exclude any firm which is not a member,
and does not accept their scale of selling charges. In
fact, they could raise their commission much higher if
they chose, and compel the woolgrower to pay (hat rate
if he sold locally, simply because wool-sales not held under
their auspices would, as matters now stand, have no com-
petition. The buyers do not care, as they are not affected
in the least—they merely wish to study their own con-
venience in attending one central mart. The outside co.
to whom you refer had no alternative but to join, in the
interests of its own business. It is monstrous that the
principal industry of the province should be thus arbi-
trarily controlled by a few firms hack'd by English capi-
tal, who grind divs. out of the producer. Most of the so-
called squatters are only unpaid managers, and their
wool is mortgaged to the big firms aforesaid, who have
the right to sell it themselves. The worst of it is that
now the free man has to submit to the same charges as
the man who is mortgaged. Here is an example of trades-
unionism from the very people who try to crush the
shearers. What about their boasted freedom cf contract?
Can you suggest a remedy ?—Yours, Unreconstructed.
• • • «
Thomas El’is and Muntz, of the late lamented
Mercantile Bank, two more of the fallen boomers of Mel-
bourne, applied for certificates in the Bankruptcy Court,
on Friday. Neither of them has paid, or is likely to pay,
the statutory 7s. in the £, and so could only claim their
discharge bv proving that they were the victims of un-
misfortune—which they didn’t do. Judge
Holro.vd, however, said that he was “bound by the Full
Court’s recent decision in the case of Matthew Davies,”
and granted both applications. Matthew is a man of
far-reaching consequences, and hecanse he got his certi-
ficate it appears that every other boomer is to do like-
wise. The law requiring an insolvent to pay 7s. in the 6
to his creditors, or show good cause why he can’t do it,
may now be regarded as abolished, so far as concerns
large, influential bankrupts. Mat. Davies found the
statute in his way, and repealed it in his own calm,
unofficial fashion.

Another ,£2O div. paid away by Drysdale gold
mine, being the 15th since Aug., ’93. Mine looks as well
as ever.
• • • •
Although the Mt. Drysdale auriferous forma-
tion is 7ft. wide all the richest gold is in a vein only 6 to 7
in. thick, carrying a lot of chloride of silver. All ore is
soon to be treated locally; the best of it, because of its
silver contents, has hitherto been sent to Wallaroo.
* ♦ • ♦
Mt. Drysdale field is said to be in many respects
remarkably like Pamhnla. The gold, though yet free
from arsenical pyrites, is contained in a conglomerate of
ferruginous slate and sandstone, intermixed with quartzite
without walls, the strike of the pay-shoot being arbitrary.

does banking-work in Australia contrast with that of
England.

The crack Hobart whaler, Helen, has dropped
into port with 51 tons sperm oil as the result of six
months’ cruising out of the 18 months’ articles. The ship
has also nearly 901 b. of ambergris on board, taken from
two whales which were trled-out by the Helen and her
sister-ship, the Waterwitch, at Port Davey (Tas.). The
present London market value for ambergris is from £2 to
£3 10s. per ounce.

Editor Bulletin, —In a par. in your issue of
22nd Nov. you say that Mr. Christmas, who tried the
Pamhula stone (with his patent metal-extractor) “ owns
to a failure.” From this it might he inferred that the
Christmas process was a failure. Let me say that I
obtained the stone sent on for trial, and that Mr. Christmas
failed to obtain more than about 6dwt. to the ton. As this
did not altogether satisfy those connected with the mine, I
obtained a second lot of stone and had a sample of it
assayed —the result of which was a little over 6dwt. The
remainder was put through “The Christmas Metal
Extractor” and the result obtau ed was about the same
as results of assay. So far, all tests have been most
satisfactory, and, under the circumstances, it will cost
under 4dwt. per ton to work the machine. Results are
anxionslv looked forward to from the Red Castle mines
in Victoria, where the inventor is working both patents :
the fine gnld-sav ing machine and the process for extrac-
tion of gold from antimony.—Yours, E. W. Christmas.

Star of the East (Hannan’s): “ The stone shows

* • • •
Work is giving Coolgardie more water than the
weather. The well in the centre of the town is yielding
1400 gal. a day. Further finds of “ fresh water ” have
been made on Hampton Plains. This talk of “fresh
water ” is premature. Nearly all water struck at Cool-
gardie is drinkable when found, hut rapidly becomes salt
as it is drawn upon. First it is “ fresh water”— i.e., Cool-
gardie water—a little worse for a Sydneyite than that of
the “ Beautiful Harbor.” Then it is good camel water,
that is pretty dense in salt and other impurities—finally,
it is fit only for the condensers.

booming. „ , , ,
From Coolgardie Miner, Nov. 17:—
Public Notice.—Any person or persons who have at
any time done me the slightest favor, such as lending me
a match, a pipe of tobacco, passing the time of day,
swapped dogs, invited me to a slice of damper ; and any
person imagining that they are thereby entitled to an in-
terest in my share in tbe Last Chance must lodge their
claim on or before Christmas Day next or for ever after
hold their peace.— Alfred Parker.

Strange are the vicissitudes of mining. At
Gilberton, on the Etheridge (says Sellheim), 80 lons from
a certain claim gave 1640 z. gold. The ground was shortly
afterwards abandoned as unprofitable. The next man
who came took 2510 z. from 41 tons and then left in dis-
gust. Had these finds been in Westralia !

S.M. Herald appears sorry that driver
Williams was not found guilty of manslaughter
in connection with the late Redfern railway
disaster. “The verdict,” it laments, “practically
says there’s no one to blame.” The verdict says
no such thing, practically or any other way. It
merely says that Williams, on the evidence, was
not guilty of manslaughter. The notion that if a
jury does not hang or imprison someone for a
disaster there is no one to blame is peculiarly
primitive.
George Reid (already “ Sir George Reed ”
in one of the English weeklies) is careful to put
in bis article on Imperialism in the Review OF
Reviews nothing that will prejudice the title
which at the bottom of his podge he hankers for.
Yet it cannot be exactly pleasant to Downing-
street to be told that its attitude to Australia is
one of “Quaker-like forbearance,” “almost
deferential.” George goes un to say that the oidy
strain on the Imperialistic “painter” will come
in war-time, and exhorts Rosebery and Salisbury
to see that their quarrel is good “in the sight of
heaven,” —Georgey is a parson’s son—otherwise
they will get no help from Australia.

Mr. Francis Abigail is, in many respects, a remarkab'e
man. Born in Hi-hnpsga!e half a century ago, he lost lis
father at 1(5 years of age, and the Inn , lame and scarcely
able to read and write, had to make his own way in the
world. This he has most successfully accomplish d. in-
got apprenticed to the boot-lasting trade, hut l> fore hi
time expired he resolved to go to Australia. Thi ty ree
years ago he landed in Sydney with a sick hroiher ;• d
the proverbial sixpence. In IS9O he visited th ■ old
country for the first time since he left these shores ; hut
he returned as an influential member cf the New South
Wales Parliament, an ex-Minister of the Crown, the prin-
cipal of a large commercial house, and Ihe chairman of
many important companies. Truly a noble example of
what industry, combined with brains, can achieve over all
obstacles.
And now ! Alas for poor Abigail and his vain
endeavors to break gaol! Truly, as Emile
Souvestre says, “one can outlive everything but a
good reputation.”
The Brisbane J’s.P. over ruled Police-
Magistrate Finuock’s refusal of bail to the men
concerned in the alleged labor kidnapping case at
Brisbane. The J.’sP. were possibly rig! *• for
papa P. is getting past work. Neverthelets, the
“cheap’’-beak system is rotten to the core, at d
J.’sP. should not be allowed to sit when a P.M.
is available. Or, being allowed to sit they should

Mr. Thomas W. Purse, whose portrait
appears in this issue, is secretary of the Sydney
Eight Hours Demonstration Committee, and the
man who is mainly responsible for the financial suc-
cess of Labor’s annual holiday. In 1892 that show
panned out for a profit of .£739. In ’93, when the
bank and other failures had sat down like a night-
mare on the province and the gate-money fell off
by about .£lOOO, the demonstration still con-
trived to pay its way and show a balance of
£32. And in ’94, when the annual function
seemed to be still on the down-grade in some
respects, it managed to produce a profit of £BOO.
On each occasion the committee reported that the
results achieved were very largely owing to the
tact, energy, and financial ability of the serious-
looking man whose portrait decorates this page,
and there is no doubt that the commendation
was well-deserved. A financier who continues, as
Mr. Fnrse has done, to show a surplus in any line
of business, for three consecutive years, deserves
to have his portrait inserted, that others may
know him arid follow in his footsteps.
'J he destruction of the rabbit, the problem
par excellence of the Australian sheepl reeder,
squatter and agriculturist, has now reached a
satisfactory conclusion. This has been achieved
through the inventive genius of Mr. Marshall, of
Dunedin, N.Z., who has hit upon a poison which
he has named Toxa, and which has proved an
infallible agent in the destruction of the long-eared
pest. Fullest evidence supports this fact. Sir
Charles Clifford, of N.Z., has cleared his run of
rabbits and added 30,000 slieep to his flock.
Thirty rabbits eat the gra-s required for one
sheep, and one rabbit killed in the breed-
ing season (summer) is equal to a dozen
killed in winter. Half -a - crown’s worth of
Toxa will kill 550 rabbits. These are some of the
statistics of the crusade. Toxa has been tested
in each colony with equal success. Recognising
its value as an aid to the pastoral and agricultural
industries, South Australia and Tasmania have
remitted their respective duties of 10 and 20 per
cent, on Toxa, and Vic. has placed no tariff
hindrance in the way of the free and general use
of the drug. N.S.VV. alone imposes an import
duty of 10 per cent. —an amusing inconsistency,
seeing how much the rabbit pest is costing the
country. The National Wool Co. of Australia,
Ltd., are the sole agents for Toxa in Australia and
Tasmania.

Phil. May’s brother, Charles May, is now
collaborating with him in black-and-white.

ornamental alias of the N.S.W. Government whip.

a century by the late ex-Justice Alfred Stephen,

Ballarat and St. Kilda, left £234,300 - £66,800
real estate and £167,500 personalty.
Kate Marsden, the lady who worked the
famous Siberian leper grab, is described as “a tall
and prepossessing woman, with a hypnotic eye.’
Montrose, the Maoriland press-
man, used to relate how, at Sheppartoo
(Vic.)agricultural show,acommitteeman
suddenly inquired, “’Scuse me, mister,
but are you a short-horn reporter ? ”
J. S. Robinson, the Johannes-
burg multi-millionaire (says The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent), enter-
tains largely at Park Lane, London,
where he (or his wife) has leased a
mansion. They are in good hands—
one of the numerous branches of the
Lennoxes —and have “the best of
people ” at their dinners and dances.
They have been wise enough to fe,el
their way gradually, unlike a well-
known Australian with a somewhat
similar cognomen, who thought to
capture the “ nobs ” by giving a huge
ball ere anybody had even heard his
name. Robinson, who is an anti-
Rhodesian, his fortunes being linked
with those of the Transvaal Republic,
lately lent the Transvaal Govt, a
million sterling to enable it to secure
the Delagoa Bay railway, which has
been done. Rhodes had previously
made an offer of even a larger amount,
which was refused with thanks a
great blow to the Cape dictator whose
dream is to join the whole of South
Africa in a Bund in which the English
will be paramount. The extension of
the Delagoa Bay railway will benefit
Natal, which hitherto has been a kind
of Cinderella, and which Rhodes has
tried his level best to smother. But
the Transvaal people are friendly with
the Natalese, who supplied most of the
money for developing the gold-fields,
and who actually own the only mines
which are paying ; most of those which
were floated in England being of the
shady order.
The late Captain MJntosh, of the lost
Wairarapa, was a man of literary and scientific
tastes, and his cabin-library was worth about
£6OO.
Sterry, Vic. M.P., has an original plat-
form attitude. When he speaks he grasps the

hand.

There’s nothing feminine in the everyday
appearance of Lord Yarmouth, the skirt-dancer
of Hobart notoriety. He is merely a bloatered
young earl with a cool, clean-shaven cheek.
Johnny Gourlay could give him points in a beauty-
competition.
John Goodman, who succeeded H. C. E.
Childers at Vic. Customs, was the roughest cus-
tomer who ever sat in the Ministerial chair. He
used to receive deputations with a short black
pipe in his mouth and cutting up black tobacco to
put in it. He would hail the visitors with “Now,
then, what’s up? Spit it out!” One day an
angry deputy exclaimed, “Excuse me, Mr. Com-
missioner of Customs, I’m no judge of your cus-
toms, maybe; but I can judge your manners, and
they’re damned bad 1”

putting of Mrs. Mellon up to the chin in a bag as
a preliminary to diagnosing the honesty of her
professions,

The Marquis of Caermarthen, who has
come to inspect this beastly country, is a raw
youth of about 25 years. When returned for
Brixton (London) a few years ago his face was
guiltless of hair, but he is now a bearded pard.
There is nothing remarkable about him, unless it
be his absolute political ineptitude.
Millionaire Tyson realises that he is being
overtaken by old age. Giving his reasons for not
speaking in Queensland Legislative Council, he
said: —“Mister” (he always calls a man who
wears a coat “Mister ”), “I am getting deaf, but
never properly knew it until I entered the House.
I can’t hear a word when the House is sitting.”
F. A. Packer, the recently-retired clerk of
Tasmanian Assembly, and composer of several
well-known songs, wrote the cantata “Land of
Beauty ” for Tas. Exhibition opening. Mr.
Packer’s mother was a daughter of Nathaniel
Gow, composer of “Caller Herrin’,” and grand-
daughter of Neil Gow, Scotland’s national
musician.
It puzzles many people why “ Bill ” Long,
who is so fond of the bawbees, and so seldom wins
a race, sticks to racing. A friend of Long’s,
knowing “Bill’s” father’s antipathy to horse-
racing, asked him one day, long ago, how he came
to take to the sport. “Because it is the only
game I can see any money in,” was the candid
reply of the M.L.C.
Rev. E. A. Anderson’s appointment to
the bishopric of Riverina has much of its social
gilt rubbed off by the announcements of the
Gunthers, Langleys and others that they would
not take the billet. By the way, ih- see of
Riverina means hard work and small pay for a
bishop, which is probably why many have failed to
hear a divine call.
Re the Denbigh Feildings : Fielding, the
novelist— who belonged to the family—was asked
by his contemporary earl why he put the “i”
before the “e?” “Because, my lord, my branch
of the family has learned to spell! ’’ The Earls
of Denbigh possess the unique (British) distinc-
tion of being Princes of the Holy Roman Empire,
and the recently-arrived General Feilding, of Q.
Transcontinental fame, is the man who was so
laughed at for his preposterous “What to do with
Our Boys.”
Mr. G. C. Henderson, 8.A., of Sydney,
has gained the Brackenbury scholarship for
modern history at Balliol College, Oxford. The
“ Brackenbury ” is the chief scholarship in modern
history open to Oxford undergraduates, and is of
the value of £BO per annum. Mr. Henderson
lately graduated as B.A. in Sydney with first-
class honors in history and philosophy, and he
now holds the James King, of Irrawang, travel-
ling scholarship awarded by the Sydney Univer-
sity, as well as the Fraser history scholarship.
He was lately a student of the Training College
under the N.S. W. Department of Public Instruc-
tion, and holds rank as a teacher in the depart-
ment.
Barring perhaps Tiger Inglis, the N S.W.
statesman, Victorian Minister Peacock has the
loudest and most hideous laugh south of the line.
“ The great Kingfisher of Australia,’’ remarks the
Banker’s Magazine (Vic.), “makes a formid-
able noise, and so does a steam saw-mill; but if
the two were united in one blend, the smile of
Mr. Peacock would surpass it in sweetness and
volume.”
Graves, M.P. for Delatite, Vic., relates
that he was once opposed by D. C. O’Leary, a
grandson of the great Dan. O'Connell, and cousin
to the O’Donoghue. Graves would have been
beaten, but for the fact that the O’Donoghue at
the time of the election had voted in the Commons
against Parnell. The Irish in Delatite, therefore,
made a dead set against his relative, who was
beaten badly by Graves,
George Melrose, the S.A. squatter who
died recently worth .£250,000, began life with 100
ewes, worth in the aggregate about £5, which
were given to him by the squatter for whom he
acted as shepherd. The late Lachlan M‘Bean
received 500 ewes as his guerdon for a similar
service. Crown lands for pasturage were cheap
and plentiful in S.A. in those days, and fences
were few and far between.
Tennyson Smith, the Gough-phonograph,
got an awful slap in the eye while in Melb. He
went to the office of the Tribune, the beer-trade
organ, and asked why they didn’t notice his meet-
ings and slang him. “ Because you’re only a
cheap showman,” grunted the editor. “I am not
a showman,’’ indignantly exclaimed the long-
tressed ranter. Then the tired editor : “ Then
why the devil don’t you get your hair cut ? ”
Rev. G. Pratt, who died last week a* s
Woollahra (N.S.W.), was, in the course of his 77
years, preacher, teacher, medicine-man, com-
pounding chemist, linguist, and author. He left
England for Samoa in ’3B, and, with the exception
of a short period, spent his whole life since about
the islands. Some of his Scripture translations
are now in the hands of Sydney printers, and his
grammar, dictionary, and folklore are all valu-
able.
Some faithful soul placed a wreath on
Nero’s tomb, and even Pastor Talmage has his
admirers. When he called for his account at
Menzies’ Hotel, Melbourne, it was sent up re-
ceipted, with a polite message that the proprie-
tress considered it an honour to have had the
famous preacher under her roof. The best sitting-
room in the hotel had also been placed at the dis-
posal of Talmage and son. This hospitality
is probably unexampled in Australian “ show ”
records.
Sir James Fergusson, now visiting Aus-
tralia, has been thrice married, and each time
married money. His first wife, Lady Ethel
Fergusson, died of typhoid fever during her
husband’s governorship of S.A. Sir James then
married Miss Richman, adopted daughter of
squatter Hughes, the latter’s dowry to the bride
being <£70,000. The second wife died in India of
cholera, and about three years ago a third Lady
Fergusson succeeded her in the person of the
wealthy widow of a London clergyman. The
youngster who received twelve months last week
for burning down a wing of his college is probably
a son of the second wife, and, with his brother,
inherits £70,000 on coming of age.

will have to find some new coin for tipping Royal

Echuca way, but something better is expected at
Pall Mall.
Attorney-General Byrnes (Q.), long the
despair of mothers with marriageable daughters,
has at last fallen a victim to a little Brisbane lass
well-known in musical circles. The young lady’s
family are of the Church of England, but report
hath it that she has recently undergone a change
of views. Byrnes is a good Catholic, and the
faithful swear by him.
Photo, by Thos. Adcock] [Adelaide.
R. HOMBURG, M.P.
Outside the Ministry, perhaps the strongest
man in S.A. Assembly is the Teutonic ex-Attorney
General Homburg, who has washed his hands of
Opposition obstruction.
Yarmouth, the skirt-dancing earl, described
by a mordant humorist as “ the latest British
bounder,’’ is badly wanted by divers Melbourne
charities to uncover his noble calves in the sacred
cause. The Melb. Hospital committee thinks
that if he could be prevailed upon to skirt-dance
for the benefit of their institution, “society”
would eagerly respond.
The Full Court hearing of Speight v. Syme
must go over till March or April. Judge Hodges
heard one case, Williams the second, Madden was
counsel for one of the parties at an earlier stage,
and Hood is away on leave. So there are only two
Judges available till Hood comes back. Then we
will hear whether the jury’s verdict was really
for plaintiff or for defendant.
Of 14 professors at Melb. Uni-
versity, only three—Elkington, Allen
and Kernot —were raised there. But
10 lecturers out of 21—to wit, Gregr ry,
Cussen and Duffy in the law school,
Smith, Henderson and Fowler in the
engineering school, Springthorpe,
Cherry, Hall and Kirkland in the
medical school—were trained wh<re
they teach ; so were the six medical
demonstrators.
Old John Nimmo, ex-M.L.A.
(Yic.), has dead against champagne
lunches at Royal Commissions. The
Yan Yean Commission visited Fe n-
shawe once. John took a pannikin of
water from the stream and sat apart
at lunch. Noticing the guide refuse a
drink, John rose up and congratulated
him on his abstinence principles.
Then the guide whispered: “You
see, it doesn’t do for me to drink. I
keep a pub.” Old John = to’e away, a
broken man.
Mr. Justice Bundey, who has
sat on Adelaide Supreme Court Bench
since ’B4, the latest change in its con-
stitution, has never taken a day’s
holiday from his judicial duties. The
Chief Justice recently enjoyed a
European and American trip for 15
months on full pay, and Mr. Boucaut
had a year’s holiday immediately
afterwards. Mr. Bundey is the only
Judge who never pushes himself into
any public affair, and who rigidly
eschews all business entanglements-
Both Mr. Way and Mr. Boucaut are
squatters, and the former essays to be
a leader of society also.
Poor old “ Jolly Sir John
Astley ” (writes a | Bulletin corre-
spondent) has gone to a world which
it is hoped will prove a better one
for him than this. A sportsman to
the tips of his fingers, he was all that
was chivalrous, generous, and kind.
He was a great admirer of Australian
scullers, and never failed to enquire
for Trickett whenever I met him. It
is not generally known that he in-
sisted on finding a share of Laycock’s
stake, with Lord Londesborough,
when the long Australian rowed Boyd
on the Tees. To Trickett he always
referred as one of the straightest men
he had ever met, though he could
never be brought to believe that Ned
could hold a candle to Hanlan.
The worst disaster which has yet fallen
upon Major Bartrop, J.P., &c., whose conduct is
now being investigated in Melbourne, was Justice
Hodges’allusion to him as “one Bartrop.” A
man never really knows how far he has dropped
until he hears himself publicly described as
“one Bartrop.” The Civil Service Board has
found the charges against the Major unproven.


WHAT JIMMY D„ THE NEW
M.P., SAYS.
In a recent interview, one of the most_ successful
newly-fledged M.’sP. was asked by an inquisitive
reporter what move be considered had most con-
tributed to his election. “No particular move
whatever,” was the answer. “I only gave my
electors to understand that I am a practical ana
an honest man ; that as I know the art, of living
within my income the same art might als > ie use-
ful to the Government, if I had my siy m the
House. To illustrate my opinion, I simply drew
forth a packet of Frossard’s Cavour Cigars, saying
that these were the cigars I always smoked ; my
purse could afford more expensive opes, but my
practical sense told me that a similar mild and
aromatic smoke could not be obtained at less than
sixpence each, wliereas these Cavours are sold
everywhere in packets of eight for one shilling.

S.A. Legislative Council widened the
exemption under the absentee-tax so as to include
the whole of Australasia, the original intention of
the Govt, being to charge everyone outside South
Australia. Squatter Duncan moved for the sub-
stitution of “ the British Empire.”
A village settlement on the absentee prin-
ciple was established by the Patterson Govt, at
Miepoll, near Enroa (Vic.). The “ settlers ” have
not settled. They take work and board and
lodging and waves from neighboring dairymen—
and subsistence-money from the Govt.
Unsatisfactory anomaly : An uncertificated
insolvent may be a N.S.W. M.P., but he cannot
be an alderman. An alderman lately retired from
a council-table on account of having filed his
schedule, and a few days afterwards presented
himself as a candidate for Parliament, and was re-
turned.
The Parkes star steadily wanes. The
other day, J. F. Cullen, “the Willoughby
Squeaker,” an ex-clergyman who had resigned his
seat on account of insolvency, presented himself
for re-election and was beaten by E. M. Clarke,
anti-Parkesite. Mr. Cullen was the gentleman
who used to hold Parkes’ books of reference
during the old man’s Assembly speeches.
It is all cut-and-dried that when Mr.
Justice Foster (N.S.W.) retires, which may be any
day, Attorney-General Simpson is to go on the
Bench and “Jack” Want is to take his place as
Attorney-General. Mr. Want, as per his letter
to tiie press, is a whole-souled advocate of the
system of judge’s-relations practising before their
Honors, and so will be very handy in the Assem-
bly as a champion of the existing order of things.

The veteran N.S.W. Agent-General Saul
Samuel cannot be disposed of so easily as N.S.W.
members appeared to think the other night. Ho
can be deposed only on pension, as he comes under
the provisions of the Civil Service Act; and t ie
present is no time to appoint a potentate with the
enlarged salary Bibbs foreshadows, and to retire
the present occupant of office on to a cushion
stuffed with taxpayers’ bank-notes.
The M‘Kenzie want-of-confidence motion
against the Turner Govt. (Vic.) was defeated
by 55 to 33. The only hope now of the Vic.
anti-increased-hmd-tax crowd the Legislative
Council. Unhappily, in Victoria, Patman is an
elected representative of Property, and can t be
swamped like his nominee congeners elsewhere.
The only weapon at the Vic. Assembly s com-
mand is a “ tack ” to an Appropriation Bill.
A would-be N.S.W. legislator, whose peti-
tion against the sitting member was held un-
sustained by the Parliamentary Elections Com-
mittee, courteously describes that body as “ a lot
of bottle-eyed dog-wallopers.”
Reid’s N.S.W. Land and Income Tax
Assessment Bill is the lineal descendant and heir-
at-law of the existing system of party government.
By it two distinctly different measures are simul-
taneously presented to the House, and members
who want a land-tax can’t vote for it without
voting for an income-tax to which they are
opposed ; while those who yearn for an income-
tax and loathe a land tax must vote for the latter,
or surrender the hope of gaining the former.
N.S.W. Postmaster-General Cooks publi-
cation of the names of the bookmakers who, he said
(though they deny it), informed on their fellows,
and Premier Reid’s disclosure of the names of civil-
service officials who composed criticisms on their
Parliamentary ex-chiefs, are new of
official discretion which may have injurious
results. Much ‘‘information received” is the
growth of confidence in official reticence.
One of the most noteworthy features of
the existing Vic. land-tax is that it applies to
country lands only; no city boomer pays one
solitary copper under its provisions. All estates
worth less than £2500 are exempt; also all estates
of less than 640 acres. As nobody owns 640 acres
of city property all Melbourne, Geelong, Bendigo
and Ballarat are exempt. Berry’s land-tax is
about the most clumsy and iniquitous device of
the kind ever invented.
Q. Assembly has given MTlwraith leave
of absence for the rest of the session. The once
“indispensable” Thomas has hardly been seen
by anybody for months, and all sorts of weird
legends are afloat as to his condition. According
to the talk in the street, the knight of Anchen-
flower suffers from a hopeless complication of
troubles. But the one point on which all who
know nothing about him are agreed is that he will
never appear in politics again.
The S.M. Hkrald disapproved of Premier
Reid’s inclusion of a land-tax and an income-tax
in one Bill. Sydney D.T. sniggered contentedly.
Ex-Treasurer M'Millan disapproved. The D.
suddenly discovere d that “ most people who have
studied economic questions to any purpose will
have a difficulty in following the Premier to this
conclusion ” —the aforesaid inclusion. Reid may
now be expected to separate the two schemes, for
the D.T. is his bell-wether, and M'Millan is the
D.T.’s ham-squatting idol.
The Amos land resumption case —in which
the N.S.W. Railway Commissioners offered £l5OO
for 12 acres of land near Mittagong, while the
owner demanded £209,000-has been settled by
the plaintiff getting back his property. When the
land-tax comes along it is to be hoped that the
Government will remember that Mr. Amos’ piece
of land, by his own showing, is worth £209,000,
and will tax it accordingly. If it doesn’t tax it
accordingly, then it will be casting a wicked
aspersion on the owner’s veracity,

N.S.W. Legislative Council wrote itself
down a purely capitalistic machine by one little
exploit over clause 69 of the Coal Mines -Bii.
That clause provides that no prosecution shall be
instituted against owner, manager, agent, op em-
ployee, for any offence not personally committed,
except the prosecution is authorised by an in-
spector or in writing by a Minister. Ibe Council
struck out the word “employee,’ and carried t e
rest of the clause with a yell, boon after Jacob
said to Hoskins : “ We sit here in the interests of
the whole country ; the other place is > dominated
by a faction that looks only to a class.

THE TRUE INWARDNESS OF
SPOOKS.
The accompanying illustration (reproduced
from “ Further Light upon the Mysteries of
Spookland ”) gives Mr. T. S. Henry’s idea of how
“Geordie” and “Cissy” are manufactured tc
meet the spiritualassic demand for apparitions
from the other side of Jordan. Given a darkened
room, a couple of masks, and a little drapery, any
Bulletin reader can now manufacture his own
spirits in large or small quantities as required to
suit customers.
M.L. Minister for Labor Reeves gets home
on the anti-land-taxers who point every doleful
prognostication with “ Look at IVlaoriland !
The M.L. land-tax is three and a-half years old.
If, says Mr. Reeves, the farmers have suffered,
how is it that they made a clean sweep of land-
tax opponents at last year’s general election?
Whence was most capital withdrawn last year—
from Maoriland or from the smashed and smashing
hanks of Australasia—who have found it neces-
sary to prevent by law the depositors from taking
out their funds ? If capital is so scarce in Maori-
land, why are the banks reducing deposit in-
terest? And, finally, if the land-tax has ruined
Maoriland credit, how is it that her securities
stand at the top of the Australasian list on the
London Stock Exchange? Hey!

HEAR THE WAIL OF THE
LONDON ALDERMAN.
London, Nov. 28.
The Queensland turtles shipped to London to
test the market have, proved a failure, being too large
and coarse Experts say that it was a mistake to cut
the turtles up or to remove a portion of the fat.
“ Removing the fat ” is where the real grievance
comes in to an alderman.
HOP’S OWN WILDCAT COLUMN.
So far, no developments have occurred
•which render the authenticity of the startling
disclosures of Nelly Connelly and Joanna
Moran with regard to the alleged wholesale
• child murders, less doubtful.
The police have not yet struck “ pay-
dirt,” and in all probability the affair will
turn out to be another Mount Hoaxley. The
public are hereby cautioned not to take up'
shares, pending further reports.
Sharks are very plentiful this summer.
Newspapers warn people against unneces-
sary bathing.
IN DARKEST SPOOKDOM.
Mrs. Mellon has vindicated herself. She was tied up in a “ black bag,
and locked up in a wire cage. Lest the whole bag of tricks should become too
etherialised and escape through the chimney, a rope was attached, and a
heavy-weight M.P. made a very effective kellick.
Judge Windeyer was present with an impartial jury, who wer©
thoroughly satisfied. The spooks, “ Cissie,” “ Geordie,” and other
kindred spirits, left the court without a stain upon their characters.

W.C.T.U. A woman may be very meek and
lowly, but she never becomes absolutely indifferent
to the opinion of man in the matter of her legs,
and you cannot call even a member of the
W.C.T.U. a “ screw ” with impunity. It’s enough
to force her into knickerbockers just to show that
they are perfectly straight. There now !
* # *
The latest idea in trades-unions is a pro-
posed “Ministers’ Wives’ Association.” Good,
by St. Dorcas and St. Gimlet and St. Gossip! as
far as it goes. But what of the celibate clergy?
Are they, in addition to their fleshly loneliness, to
be unrepresented among the leagues of the Re-
generate New Woman? Why not a “Celibate
Parsons’ Female-Admirers’ Association ?” By the
•great carpet-slipper of the lonely sacred hearth,
■this is a weepful necessity ! Moreover, are devout
■lay males to be debarred from membership of the
“Ministers’ Wives’ Association”? A devout
'layman is often a great source of consolation and
’restfulness to a minister’s wife, and the link is holier
than any sympathetic connection ever idealised by
‘Plato. Then the corollaries of these schemes are
•endless. The soaring purity of this generation
craves for a “Ministers’ Sisters’ Association,” of
which all brothers of the churches may be honorary
and sympathetic members. Salutations to the
devout! Spread abroad the scrumptious and
holy kiss of peace ! Long may she smack !
* * *
The Social Purity section of the Presby-
terian Assembly sooled on their Chief Gimleteer
to urge the Vic. Chief Commissioner of Police to
suppress the Tableaux Vivants. In an indignant
letter the C.G. pointed out that the tableaux ex-
hibited in Collins-st. were immoral and responsible
for the increase in divorce. In reply to which
rubbish the Chief Commissioner replied that he
had not personally had an opportunity of seeing
the tableaux, but from all he could learn there was
nothing in them that could be objected to. He
further pointed out that the photographs com-
plained of were not those of the tableaux at all,
but merely of famous works of art which the
living pictures represented.
• • •
So far as The Bulletin is concerned, all
divorce-reports can be sub-edited by the W.C.T.U.,
which would no doubt dearly love the job. But
if the expurgation of divorce-reports is to operate
at all it should operate all round—not only in
the moneyed case Synnot v. Synnot, but also in
the coster case Hawkins v. Hawkins. Vic. Judge
A’Beckett must take care not to give moneyed
suitors a monopoly of his protection from the
evening press.
• # •
You now see lady-trainers at Melb. sub-
urban pony-meetings. There are only two at pre-
sent, but more are to follow. They superintend
the preparation of the ponies, saddle them up, see
that the boy weighs out, give him a leg-up, and,
after the race, rub the pony down, allesame as
a man. There are very few he-trainers, who can
give them a pound in training a pony—or in
“talk,” either.
THE NEW WOMAN: Miss HELEN
HART , lecturess, on a political campaign.
The Bulletin predicts that little more will
be seen in Australia of Mrs. Mellon as a medium
save by her intimate friends. The “ test seance,
with its nebulous results, was an affair admirably
designed to make her fall as easy as possible.
Though this so-called “ test-sdance ”
was, broadly speaking, conducted by a little_gang
of spiritualists —some of them howling hystericists,
credulous to the last degree, others, inquirers
fast getting into the dupe stage—what was the
result? Nothing, save some indefinable white
apparitions, which might have been the ghosts of
the pigs stuck at the abattoirs the night before,
just as they might have been the ghosts of the four
choked little Mount Rennie boys or the shade of
the Marble Man. Under the circs., the exultant
shriek of Dr. McCarthy in the daily press is as
pitiable as it is absurd.
The public idea of a proper test-committee
would be something like this : Three or four lead-
ing doctors—say Drs. Sydney Jones, Scot-Skirving,
Fiaschi and Angel-Money (if they had time to
devote to the investigation of such oft-exploded
shams); a couple of conjurors, two female
searchers from Darlinghurst prison, and two or
three people who, having inspected the horsehair
with which the spiritualistic cocoanut is stuffed,
have cast their delusions behind them. Then let
the cranks nominate an equal number and go
ahead.
The emotional doctor who has been the main-
stay of discredited Mellon spooks gravely counsels
believers not to be misled by impulse, but to
investigate the phenomena in a calm, judicial
manner ! Satan and Sin surely must have been
spiritualasses.

The story goes that when the sister of a
well-known North-West Australian magnate
was seduced by another prominent society spark,
the brother vowed that he would be the lover of
the seducer’s wife. He fulfilled his vow. Said
society spark afterwards remarked that he didn t
mind that so much, but when it came to all of
the Govmentouse party assisting in the vendetta
it was too much. Ho had to draw the line some-
where, so he shipped the lady home.
# * *
When the skirt-dancing Yarmouth arrived
in Melbourne last week he was immediately
attacked by a Herald interviewer—whom he re-
buffed with a cold, aristocratic stare. The Herald
came out same day with an alleged portrait of the
gifted young nobleman in which—by a slip of the
chisel —he is represented as having a ring in his
nose or else a polypus depending from his near
nostril. He seems a harmless young man enough
—at least when dressed as a male—
Why should it of this peer he said
That he no sense of shame reveals,
If, having nothing in his head,
At fame he clutches—with his heels ?

The Melbourne Women’s Christian Tem-
perance Union talks of starting a Sailors
Home. To those who know anything of the ideas
and ambitions of Jack ashore this should be
humorous reading.

Austral Salon —Astral Salon.
* # *
Board of Control President Backhouse
explains that the Pitt Town (N.S.W.) Labor
settlers co-operated “beautifully’’ as long as they
were hungry; but as soon as Jeshurun waxed
fat on good Govt, rations he began to kick the
beneficent Board. This is the same old trouble
that Mr. Bumble discovered in the case of Oliver
Twist. “It’s Meat!” Red Republicanism is
only another form of raw beef.
• * *
The yarn about one Robert Stewart, fined
for drunkenness, vainly endeavoring to “stiffen”
an editor, who next morning was rewarded for his
stringent adherence to duty by reading the name
of defendant as “Ronald Sievart,” recalls another
where the positions are reversed. A certain mag-
nate, being fined at a Police Court, approached
the single reporter present with an offer of <£lo
to keep his name out of the paper. Being re-
fused, the bribe was gradually increased to .£2O
—but the man of dots and dashes was adamant.
Next day, however, that reporter noticed that his
par. had not been inserted. Prisoner had found
it cheaper to deal with the proprietor of the paper
direct.

On the acquittal, at Brisbane, of John
Willard, N.S. Wales ex-M.L.A., on a charge of
larceny of a public document, Chief Justice
Gr iffith is reported to have remarked that Willard
“was a lucky man.” Whether Willard was or
was not lucky had nothing whatever to do with
Judge Griffith in his official capacity; and such
remarks should be resented every lb is
gratuitously insolent for any judge to criticise the
verdict of any jurv, and The Bulletin hopes yet
to find a jury-foreman, when insulted in this
fashion, telling His Honor, respectfully but
plainly, to mind his own business.
• • •
Miss Flora Esdaile, the talented young
artiste whose portrait appears in this week’s Bul-
letin, is a native of Auckland by birth, and a
Melbourneite by adoption. Her voice early gave
great promise, and at 15 she gained first prize at
Melb. Juvenile Exhibition. Signor Hazon soon
afterwards took Miss Esdaile in hand, and she
made her first appearance in public under his
auspices. Subsequently Miss Esdaile took lessons
from Madame Lucy Chambers, and afterwards
joined Nellie Stewart’s Opera Co., playing
Beatrice in “Boccaccio,” and Gaeswinda m
“Chilperic,” to Melbourne audiences. Since
then she has been in the thick of engage-
ments, having played for Gourlay in musical
comedy; for the M'Mahons in burlesque;
and for George Rignold in “ Midsummer Night s
Dream.” J.C.W.’s keen eye soon noted Miss
Esdaile’s promising quality, and she took a pan-
tomime part in “ Babes of the Wood,” afterwards
touring with the Royal Comic Opera Go., the
Gaiety Burlesquers, and the Italian Opera Co.
With the last-named in Melbourne she played
Lola in “ Cavalleria Rusticana,” and the con-
ductor was very anxious that she should come to
Italy, but at the time that was impossible.
Madame Belle Cole has advised Miss Esdaile
strongly to go to London to study, and she hopes
to leave for England early next year. In the
meantime, she is to take the loading singing part
in the pantomime at Sydney Her Majesty’s.

The chief anxiety of one of the Parra-
matta Industrial School girls, who primed the
Sydney detectives last week with stories of mur-
dered infants, was to learn what the newspapers
said of the revelations.
# * •
The fact of a certain Melbourne journal’s
special reporter being in Hobart for the Exhibi-
tion was a bad thing for the Earl of Yarmouth.
The following is the text of the reporter’s message
to headquarters when it was known what the
nature of the “ noble lord’s ” entertainment was to
be : “ The Earl of Yarmouth —a young man of 23
—is going to make a confounded ass of himself at
the Theatre Royal on Monday evening by leading
in sundry music-hall vagaries. What am I to do
to save the nobility from such a reproach ? ” And
the answer came something like this : “ Cable
full particulars, give him beans, and hang the
expense.” Hence the young man’s anger and the
salvation of the nobility !

Somebody “ had a lark ” with the Aus-
tralasian in supplying the photographs which
appeared in that paper as “ twelve Tasmanian
beauties,” The subjects were all taken from
Hobart and mostly one quarter Holebrook
Place—a tiny area wherein “society” dwelleth.
As for beauty—well, comparisons are odious.

The Hobart drapers’ and grocers’ assistants
association, which has for its object the fostering
of the weekly half-holiday movement, celebrates
this month its eleventh anniversary of continuous
cessation from work on Wednesday afternoons.
# # #
Up Rockhampton way is a station run on
somewhat peculiar lines. Nearly all the em-
ployes are black boys, and all the black boys are
opium-consumers. The astute Caucasian squatter
pays all wages in the drug, and at times the place
is a perfect Pandemonium.

The public valuation of the We-Can-
Tame-U woman was shown at Sydney local-option
poll last week, when out of 22,000 ratepayers
about 2000 bothered to record their votes.
# • #
Ex.-attachd Forbes, so far the only bagged
bird (matrimonially speaking) of the Hopetoun
suite, is in appearance a typical “Sandy Mac.,”
yellow-haired and lean, the latter characteristic
accentuated on state occasions by kilts, a costume
surely designed by some Sassenach demon
to exaggerate the angles of Bony Scotland’s
sons. The bridegroom’s liege lord and lady, smil-
ing tolerantly upon the alliance, travelled from
Wiridgil to glorify the nuptials with their pre-
sence, an honor scarcely anticipated by the over-
joyed recipients. The Hopetouns, unlike their
match-making predecessors, have hitherto arro-
gated to themselves the undivided love and
obedience of the staff, no member of which, until
lately, dared transgress the unwritten law, “Fol-
lowers not allowed.”

It has just been announced (remarks Thb
Bulletin’s London correspondent) that a Mr.
Mobbs, of N.S.W., is coming to England to pro-
secute a claim to a large estate. If he be a
claimant to the Hoxton Estate, which is now a
part of Greater London, he may as well stay
where he is. The Mobbs case is good up to a
certain point, but the one necessary link is miss-
ing, and Lord Alington cannot be ousted. The
writer knows, for he advanced money to a member
of the Mobbs family to assist in recovering the
property, and a small company was formed for
the same purpose and debentures issued, all to no
purpose. Alington draws about .£BO,OOO a year
from the property, on which his grandfather,
Humphrey Sturt, lent a couple of hundreds
only on mortgage. Those who know how care-
lessly old records were looked after will not
wonder that in all such oases it is always the most
important deed which can never be traced.
• * #
Young Tom Brown, who was married to
the eldest daughter of Chief Justice Griffith in
Brisbane on 28th Nov., is one Of the firm of D. L.
Brown and Go., and is a good type of the Scotch
Queenslander. He belongs to a strict Presby-
terian family, and his bride is grand-daughter of
the late “ papa ” Griffith, who was a pronounced
Nonconformist and minister of the Congregational
Church in Wharf-street, Brisbane. The match
attracted so much “ society ’’attention in Brisbane
that St. John’s Anglican Church was deemed the
proper place for the wedding.
» # •
Why doesn’t R. S. Smythe organise a
fresh young concert-company from the heap of
native talent now available? He has been dealing
in imported “stars” for a good many years, but
The Bulletin never heard of him trying to make
’em himself. Mrs. Armstrong was a glorious
singer before she left Melbourne, yet she was
allowed to warble gratis at Liedertafel concerts
and bazaars, and other haunts of amateurism,
until the public couldn’t have been persuaded that
they were listening to Madame Melba. Wanted
a manager with faith in his own judgment, who
will get hold of any first-class local article before
the name becomes well-known, and spend £lOO in
advertising the greatness of his discovery. It is
absurd to argue that local artists can’t be
“starred.” Remember Essie Jenyns.
• * •
The other day an elderly woman suicided
at Redfern (Sydney) through “family misfor-
tunes.” She had two daughters ; the husband of
one went to South Africa in search of employ-
ment, and his infant died in his absence. Then
news arrived of the husband’s death, and on hear-
ing this the young widow went out and attempted
suicide on her child’s grave. Shortly after this
the husband of the other daughter became a
lunatic and had to be removed to an asylum. The
rest of the family were mostly out of work, and
so, at last, the poor mother of all these misfortunes
arose early one morning and sawed her head
nearly off.
« » •
The balance-sheet of the Australasian
Institute of Journalists is a melancholy one.
Members’ subscriptions received, .£36 ; outstand-
ing ditto, £206. Only 10 pressmen attended the
annual meeting. The institute is practically dead.
* • *
The itinerant camera fiend was among the
first to get to the scene of the Wairarapa wreck,
and all the interesting corpses—the ones whose
photos, were likely to sell—were “ taken ” before
burial. They were chiefly girls and women, and
most of them were bruised and horribly dis-
figured. A good business is being done in these
gruesome relics, and apparently there is no law to
stop the ghoulish trade.

• • •
Dear Bulletin, —The most pathetic illus-
tration of the depression in Melbourne which 1
have yet met takes the shape of a man of a
Hittite cast of countenance, whom I used to notice
about the streets some months ago, admire
because of his magnificent black c> . -which
suggested the curled and oiled Assyrian bull. I
saw him again the other day. His curls were
black no more ; their hue was sickly grey. Hard
times —no money for hair-dye. Tears came to my
eyes as I looked at him. Judge how his heart
must have been wrung ! — Creeve Roe.

* # •
Sydney harbor is getting along nicely.
About the most picturesque part of the Lane Cove
River is artfully disfigured with a starch mill and
a piggery. A beautiful reach of the Parramatta
is adorned with a large, reddish, smellful gas-
works. The wide stretch of water where the Par-
ramatta and Lane Cove fall into the harbour is
decorated with an iron-foundry and a kerosene
bond ; and a wool-store glares forth on Kirribilli
Point. And now a specially smellful bone-mill
has been started in Kerosene Bay, and, on a hot
day, the sickly odour of crushed bones pervades
Balmain, Drummoyne, Greenwich, Darling Har-
bour. and all Iron Cove. There are only about
half a dozen of these eye-sores, all told, but they
are so judiciously spread, and so carefully planted
on the most lovely spots available, that it is hardly
possible not to be in sight or in smell of one of
them. About the only place of refuge soon will
be Middle Harbor, where, by the way, a thought-
ful corporation has erected nightsoil-dessicating
works.

been instructed by a Gbinainan to apply for a

manded at the last moment. “Times too welly
bad,” said the heathen, flinging down a florin ;

some time.” “But I don’t drink!” “Nevee
mi’, that for youselluf you likee. You buyee
flout, eatee longa me.”

LOCAL EVIDENCE THIS.
Mr. Thomas Edgeworth, Binalong, who writes
on June 12th, 1893: —About twelve months ago
I was a great sufferer from indigestion. Tin
symptoms from which I have suffered were great
pains in the chest, especially after meals. I had
no sooner eaten anything than I would have a
violent fit of retching ; I could not retain anything
on my stomach; the consequence was that 1
became weaker daily, although I had the advice
of a doctor, and also his medicine ; I derived no
benefit from either. Seeing so much about Clem-
ents Tonic in every newspaper I took up, I resolved
to get a bottle. I did so, and by the time I had
finished the third bottle I was thoroughly cured
of all disagreeable symptoms. If ever I feel out
of sorts, I fly to Clements Tonic, and I am happy
to say I am never disappointed. If I was to write
for a month I could not say more in favour of
Clements Tonic, though I am a thorough believer
in it. —I remain, yours sincerely, Thomas Edge-
worth, fencer, Binalong.

* * "
The trustees of Sydney Cricket Ground
are to be sympathised with over the financial
results of the match. Stoddart’s team v. N.S.W.
A total gate of £2376 was a poor return for the
public to make for the trustees’great enterprise
and foresight. This sum was only got by the
most meritorious economies in cutting off the
usual brass band, the abolition of checks during
luncheon, and retrenchment in bunting. The
20,000 people on Saturday and 5000 on each of the
other days were delighted to sit out in the broiling
sun and offer up thanks to the trustees for not
spending a few of the contributed shillings in
planting trees for shade-purposes.

* # *
Dr. Came Ross, of Manchester (Eng.),
j s Ba id t 0 have made an interesting discovery—
that ordinary cinnamon has a remarkable cura-
tive effect in cancer cases. He boils one pound of
ordinary cinnamon-stick in three pints of water in
a closed vessel for eight hours, by which time the
decoction is reduced to one pint. Half-a-pint of
the mixture, unstrained, is administered to the
patient each day in suitable doses, the result in
the five cases in which it has been tried being that
all pain ceased, and there was a marked improve-
ment in general health.

The lawyers are saying that there are more,
bigger, stronger and livelier fleas in N.S.W.
divorce court than in any other local legal haunt
of vermin. Whether these agile nippers and
bounders come into the court from out of conceal-
ment in the petticoats inseparable from the
proceedings, or whether they appear in court in
order to select among the petticoats, is at present
unsettled.

v The surveying profession feels the depres-
sion very severely. Sydney surveyors recently
tendered at from ,€22 to .£llO for a detail survey
near Bogolong, and the surveyor tendering at £29
received the contract. Granted fine weather he
has three weeks’ field-work before him and two
weeks’ office-work, and so, if Providence be pro-
pitious, he can hope to make the wages of an
ordinary camp-cook. Another recent tender was
for making out 16,000 graves for the Wesleyan
Cemetery Trust, at Rookwood, Sydney. Nearly
all the surveyors tendered at 6d. or 7d. a grave,
but the job fell to the man whose tender was 2|d.
There are, by the way, some 13,000 Wesleyans
already interred at Rookwood.
Christmas is a veritable god-send to the
Sydney papers. One paper advertises a “free
gift Christmas-box ” of £lo, to be balloted for
by those of its readers who care to write
their names on a coupon, and send it to the office
together with “one penny stamp.” Which means
that when the first “ 2000 constant subscribers ”
send in their coupons, the paper will have raked
in its £lO, and every coupon thereafter with the
penny stamp will go into the proprietor’s pocket.
Truly a safe and ingenious Christmas-box !
Dear Bulletin, —Touching the strychnine
v. chloride of lime controversy in connection with
snake-poisoning. In the first place, snake-venom
may kill in two ways. If a fatal dose be rapidly
Photo, by Vandyck ] [ Melbourne.
Dr. MUELLER, of Yackandandah, Vic.,
propounder of the strychnia remedy for
snake-bite.
injected directly into the blood, i.e., through the
wall of a vein or artery, the blood immediately
clots, and death ensues from the clots filling up
the chambers of the heart and so stopping its
action. In this case, of course, the blood is
coagulated after death. But if, on the other
hand, a fatal dose be injected under the skin, but
not directly into a vein or artery, a chemical
alteration takes place in the blood, due, possibly,
as Professor Halford believes, to the presence of
some ferment in the venom. This altered blood,
circulating through the nervous system, has a
deleterious effect upon it, reducing its power bit
by bit, and so gradually slowing down the
heart’s action till death ensues from heart-
stoppage. In this case, the blood is fluid
after death. In the second place, the actions
of the two alleged antidotes are entirely different.
The chloride of lime acts chemically upon the
venom, decomposing it and so preventing it from
acting on the blood. Strychnine, however,
does not act on the venom at all, but is a
stimulant which aids in keeping the heart
going till the poison is worked out of the
system by the excretory organs. Now, a com-
parison of the two actions of the venom, and of
the actions of the alleged antidotes, suggests three
inferences ; (a) that in the case of direct injection
of venom into a blood-vessel, chloride of lime
would be almost, it not quite, useless on account
of the extreme rapidity with which venom acts
when mixed with a quantity of blood ; (b) that
strychnine would in the same case be absolutely
useless, because, no matter how much the
heart may be stimulated, it can no more
pump clotted blood than an ordinary well-
pump can lift half-cold tar or mud ; (c)
that, in the case of injection under the skin,
but not directly into a vein or artery, both the
lime-salt and the strychnine would be more or less
efficacious, for, owing to hypodermically-injected
venom taking a longer time to work through the
tissues into the blood, the lime-salt would have
any amount of opportunity to decompose the
venom before it had much chance to do harm,
while the strychnine, by stimulating the heart,
would keep the patient alive while the lime-salt
was at work. It is therefore not impossible that
the most useful treatment would be to utilise both
alleged antidotes. Now, as to Dr. Mueller, no
one doubts his statement that all snake-bitten
persons treated by his strychnine method exactly
as he directs have recovered. But to argue
from those cases that strychnine is a true
antidote is illogioal, being an illustration of
the fallacy of post hoc ergo propter hoc. Dr.
Mueller’s argument is shortly this—bite, strych-
nine, recovery ; therefore strychnine is an anti-
dote to snake venom. There are three possible
qualifying circumstances which he totally ignores,
(a) the snake’s poison-gland may have been empty
at the time of biting; (6) the amount of poison
injected may have been less than a fatal dose ; (c)
some animals, and possibly, therefore, some persons,
are more or less immune for various reasons. The
only certain and effective way to settle the matter
would be to inject venom into 30 well-grown
healthy men—into a bloodvessel in 15 cases and
hypodermically into the remainder. Then treat
five from each set with the lime-salt only, another
five of each set with strychnine only, and the re-
maining five of each set with both the lime-salt
and the strychnine. The results would give an
absolutely final verdict. But, as these experi-
ments would be legally thirty direct attempts to
murder, they are not likely to be performed.—
Yours truly, Steele Grey.

846 Macquarie-streeb North, Sydney,
December 4, 1894,
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
Gov. Duff and family got away from Sydney on
the hottest day locally experienced in Nov. since
—well, long before I was born. It is an indica-
tion of the panting new-chum, by the way, that
he always makes a break for green hills afar off
on a day when the weather goes one better than
Tophet, instead of calmly sitting down on a wet
cabbage-leaf and putting off his Hegira until the
Mercury comes down a rung or so. The vice-
regal interest is withdrawn to Moss Yale decently
and according to the regulations. That is one
great comfort about the Duffs—they go round the
good old V.R. circus-ring without wanting to
jump on the cushions, so to speak—whereas the
Jerseys were for ever running wide of the social
peg-marks. One summer, “M.E.J.” neglected
Moss Vale and toured Maoriland, and the society
folks, in what has been called the Simla of
N.S.W., pined in vain for small vice-regal tea-
parties under the shadeless gum-trees. By the
way, Governor Duff means to make fortnightly
visits to town from his Arcadian retreat, so that
the Hie’lan’ Society may catch his Excellency yet.
A distinguished personage who still yearns in
vain for a son and heir must have had uncommon
moral fortitude several years ago to refrain from
doing the warming-pan trick when a flourishing
boylet was charged to his account by an un-
principled woman. The wild oat is now a
flourishing youth—but the opportunity to palm
him off on the lawyers has gone for ever.
It seems to me that the upper classes lose grit
nowadays, and wouldn’t know what to do with
the bowl and dagger of the Middling Ages. What
a romance in real life the world might have had if
that illegal infant had been smuggled into my
lady’s chamber ! Then the conventional sequel—
the haughty potentate confessing on his death-bed
that the young Asterisk had not a strawberry-
mark on his left arm, amid the cheering of the
next of kin. If people won’t commit a promising
crime like that what nope is there for the “society ”
paragraph ists 1
For up-to-date reading commend me to church-
reports, and if I quote this one in extenso it is
because I am so fascinated by the simple, direct
way in which it shows how lovely woman runs the
churches for all they’re worth, and to cut a syllable
would spoil it. It all occurred on the Riverina
(N.S.W.). At a meeting of the synod: “It was
recommended that a probationer be appointed to
the Adelong circuit, and an unmarried home mis-
sionary to Gundagai, the probationer to reside at
Tumut. The Narandera, Wilcannia, and Went-
worth circuits were relieved from their obligations
to take married preachers in place of single ones.
The Deniliquin circuit requested to be relieved of
its married preacher, but the request was nob
granted. The statistical returns show a general
increase. ”
It seems to me that the parson is between the
devil and the deep sea. They want to lasso
him in a sentimeutally-unbroken condition; bub
what sort of a chance has he got to preserve his
maiden modesty in the midst of howling hordes of
hook-nosed spinsters, fluffy choir-girls, and
slipper-faking ingenues, I should like to know?
And when he has killed the goose that lays the
golden eggs and sacrificed himself in the marriage-
market (as every petticoat in the district intended
that he should, or why screech for a bachelor?),
then he has the rest of his flock—for even the best-
intentioned cleric can marry but one lamb at a
time—“requesting to be relieved of its married
ineacher,” as per matrimonial advb. It takes a
ot of diplomacy for a married man to steer a
cargo of single females to the Golden Gate these
times. By the way, I read a book the other day
in which it was asserted that the only crime the
cloth never committed was arson 1 They get too
much blazes in the way of business, I suppose.
Profes-edly pious people are singularly illiberal,
and often hold up tlieir wings too high out of the
mud. There was a wicked woman on an Orient
boat at whom a righteous maiden-lady talked in
vain, until at last the worldling got restive and in
retaliation invited the saint to afternoon-tea over
corkscrews in her cabin. Bub the holy damsel
refused, poker-backed. “Very well, then,” said
tnadame, “if you won't come to my cocktail party
I won’t go to your prayer party.”
Women who are tired of life, as the quack
advts. say, oughtn’t to go governessing. There
died the other day in N.S. W. a respected instruc-
tress of youth who didn’t go hence until she had
celebrated 100 birthdays. But fancy being a
governess for 100 years more or less I Imagine
how the plucky old lady must have had to crick
her neck to keep pace with the times ! How her
list' of' accomplishments must have expanded,
beginning with the harpsichord and the sampler,
and ending up as game as a pebble with the piano
and poker-work ! And what a treasure she must
have been in the evening of her life to the proprie-
tress of a husband with a roving eye who wanted
the dear girls educated ! If all governesses were
100 years old, how pleased all the married women
would be !
The next and much-needed social thrill Aus-
tralia will get is the visit of Prince Francis
Joseph, of Battenberg, brother-in-law to Princess
Beatrice, who sails (I mean the prince does) for
Australia shortly via the land of curry-powder.
At present, Australian society is like a novel with-
out a hero. Certainly, we have a handful of
governors, but they are all married and worried,
and lack the charm of novelty, and their staffs
of well-known aides-de-camp hardly infuse any
startling interest into the social plot, though the
aides do get married now and then. Captain
Forbes, for example, whose mamma-in-law (only
she wasn’t it then) sent round the invitations in the
new style—for all the world like the prospectus
you get at breakfast from Miss Blank, teacher of
calisthenics. I cannot say I admire the new style.
Marriage is too much of a trade now-a-days for
people to serve handbills round to the invites, in-
stead of gold-edged cards. But I mustn’t forget
that I am talking about the prince.
When his Highness arrives he will supply a
long-felt want. Princes are scarce in Aus-
tralia, thank goodness, and the prints of Prince
Alfred, who arrived here long before a young
thing like me can remember, are as fresh in
some quarters as though he set sail but yester-
day. One Victorian lady, whose parents enter-
tained the Duke, tells how the girls used to go
and kiss the handle of his door in the mornings
and fight for the privilege of cleaning his boots !
If Battenberg has anything like the same luck he
ought to have a fairly expressive time in our
midst.
The Xmas annuals are upon us and give good
value for one shilling this year. Publisher
Samuels, who always has something smart on the
market, is the deus-ex-machina of the Violet
Musical Album, got up in cool purple and gold,
and containing eleven original items by popular
Australian artistes, such as Madame Kellermann,
Caron, Harrison White, Aug. Juncker, and
so on—also, there are good portraits of well-known
actresses strewn about its advertising space. The
Violet is an altogether alluring number. Paling’s
Xmas annual goes well and strong this year,
“Two Little Girls in Blue,’’ and “Simon the
Cellarer,” are alone worth the money, and a book
that gives you songs like those at less than one
penny each (there are 16 numbers) must rush over
any counter.
A most original annual is the “Fitz-Stubbs
Musical Album,” got up in Xmas colors with a
portrait of the clever composer in a medallion.
Miss Fitz-Stubbs gave a matinee in the old Sydney
Club Rooms last Friday, when the sparkling con-
tents of the Album were gone through to every-
body’s loud approval; Mr. Harry Woods, Miss Ada
Baker and Mr. Fitzmaurice singing the songs,
with Miss Fitz-Stubbs at the piano. The
“Hobart” gavotte and the New Year’9s song
and chorus went especially well. These albums
are all excellent substitutes for Xmas cards.
I lately had a little pow-wow with Mrs. Had-
docks, the lady who wheeled hence to the Cab-
bage Garden and came back per mare, but no
secrets of the bicycle were divulged during the
chat. In appearance the redoubtable Mrs. M. is
not a startling advt. for female-cycling, to my
idea. She is a graceful light-weight with a poeti-
cally pale visage, though she protests that she has
put on flesh since she started wheeling and never
felt more fit in all her born days. Of course the lady
must know her constitution better than anybody
else, yet, judging by her delicate looks, I still
think that cycling is much too violent for my poor
dear sex. Mrs. Haddocks is an enthusiastic
believer in the benefits of bicycle riding, and cer-
tainly she ought to know after making a bagatelle
of a 600 miles’ run, but for all that I will stick to
my inexperienced opinion until I meet what looks
like a really healthy girl on a gridiron.
I don’t “barrack” for women on wheels for
more reasons than hygienic ones. No really-know-
ing woman ever puts herself into an unbecoming
position for one thing, and I haven’t seen a lady
yet who looked her best on a machine. In
knickers and hose, with a cheeky little cap and
small ankles, I grant you she might look per-
fectly killing; but the average woman on a
bicycle appears to try and make her sect
as dowdy as possible —in expiation, as it
were, of her advanced appearance straddling a
“ bike”—and so comes out self-effaced in skirts like
chaff-bags. And yet, on the other hand, a
woman’s an idiot to display the shapely nether
limbs for nothing when she might earn good gold
per week doing the same thing behind the floats.
So far, however, the Australee woman pedals
round in unaggressive skirts, but I don’t think we
are very far off the knicker stage, and I am willing
to wager that I can name the lady (a Melbour-
nian) who will be the first to ride down an Aus-
tralian Block, showing the clocks on her stock-
ings.
A lady doomed to high civic honors—she wears
{mrple feathers and isn’t afeard of Govmentouse—
ately attended an afternoon tea where every-
body was talking of the way Mr. Blank and Mrs.
Dash were “ carrying ” on. The hints grew
darker and darker, until Mrs. Coming Mayoress
charitably endeavored to put a stop to the scandal.
“ I assure you,” said she, “ there ain’t no ’arm in
it. It’s quite a Plutonic attachment!”
It must be true that the youth I spoke of last
week has married a W. and M. model. Somebody
taxed him with it and he turned scarlet and denied
it. That is the odd way the average man is con-
structed. If you accuse him of matrimony he
blushes with shame, but you might throw golden-
haired ladies in his teeth for a month, and he
would only look conceited all the while.
I have seen the kinetoscope. It is impossible
to describe the ingenuity of this marvellous in-
vention, but if you look at the stage through
the reverse end of your opera-glasses some night
you will get a fairish idea of what the kineto-
scope pictures look like. It has caught on tre-
mendously in Sydney.
Mr. Ernest Hoskins, a truly gifted conjuror,
ventriloquist and “spirit” expounder, who has
shown his clever bag of tricks at Government
House, to the expressed delight of Gov. Duff,
which is saying a great deal—gave a most enjoy-
able private stance at Sydney Coffee Palace on
Monday evening, when a goodly crowd rolled up
all agog to see Mrs. Mellon’s dodges turned seamy-
side out. Mr. Hoskins is a small, fair youth like
Cupid with a basin crop, and the oalm and deadly
way in which he went to work was very soothing
to the feelings of the sceptics present. His mock
stance was most successful. People who visit the
Mellon spookeries vowed that Mr. Hoskins*
Geordiea, Josephines and Cissies were far and
away more spooky spooks than Mrs. M.’s; and,
certainly, when Josephine undulated through
the curtains she was the most convincing ghostess
a sane person could wish to see. And don’t for-
get that Mr. Hoskins produced these phenomena
tied up in a black bag like a pound of soot, with
as many ropes tied to him as if he had been a
falling tree, and sealed round the neck like a mail-
bag by apparently reliable men. From any
point of view the test was a severe one, but Mr.
Hoskins made light of his prowess. “I have been
years inquiring into spiritualism,” he said, “and
have never yet seen anything incapable of scienti-
fic explanation.” There was a good audience,
men preponderating. The much-advertised Mr.
Henry was there— but the feature of the darkened
room was the position in which a certain lady sat.
The private cricket match, Press versus Par-
liament, was hit off at Sydney Association
Ground on Monday, when the press was most
emphatically worsted ; but, as nobody took the
game seriously, it didn’t amount to much. The
awful spectacle of a large fat gentleman (on the
Parliamentary side), dressed in white flannels,
and with big hands thrust into swollen cricket-
ing gloves, turned the affair into hilarious bur-
lesque. This large gentleman’s wicket-keeping
was absolutely unique. When he wasn’t flat on
his shirt-front swimming after the ball he was
catching it in the pit of the stomach, amid the
appreciative roar of the stands. He reminds me
of Lord _ Jersey, whom I once saw turn round
and receive a cricket-ball on his spine.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
T. W. FURSE, Secretary N.S.W. Eight Hours'
Demonstration Committee.
Young Waddy, son of Mr, Percy Waddy, of
the Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney, has been elected
captain of the Balliol College cricket club, and will lead it
next season—a big distinction lor a boy who has been but
one year in England.

The New Woman, or the product of higher
education of women ii. mathematics:—The bookmaker’s
lady-clerk in the ring on Melb. Cup-day.
• • • •
The Melb. crowd failed to roll up as formerly
at the M.B.C.’s meeting, last Saturday, probably owing
to a well-founded belief that the strong wind blowing
would prevent good racing. The pace was generally
slow, and the finishes, with one or two exceptions, were
processions. The superiority of a sloped asphalt track
over the level grass one of the M.C. Ground was also
more than ever manifest, for three accidents, two of them
undeniably the result of the wheels skidding on the
slippery grass, occurred at the same spot. The other fall
occurred in the Mile Australian Championship, when
Arthur Turner, the sole Vic. representative left in the
final, and J. MTnnes (N.S.W.) came down heavily
together, the local man arising immediately to protest
against Megson, who, he alleges, crossed in front of him
without being the clear regulation length ahead. Turner’s
protest was, however, disallowed, but the crowd, nine-
tenths of whom were unable, by their position, to judge
the merits of the case, hooted Megson vigorously as he
pedalled home. Some more excusable bowlings were the
expressions of disgust levelled at all the riders in this
event for their tactics in literally crawling three-quarters
of the distance, MTnnes’ early spurt in the final being the
one laudable exception.
In the big event, four scratch men remain in the
final next Saturday, of whom Megson, on his performances
so far, is regarded by many as the deadest of dead birds.
Middleton, on the 180yds. mark, carried off his heat in a
manner which should shorten his odds considerably, while
Neil Matterson, the erstwhile sculler, who is alleged to be
backing himself vigorously (200yds), and Clinton (280yds),
were not pushed sufficiently to show their capabilities.
Taking one consideration with another, the general idea
is that the winner will oome from the above-mentioned—
1. M boson or Clinton.
2. Middleton.
3. Matterson.
J. A. MTnnes (no relation to the Sydneyite), of South
Melb., who is still left in the Austral, is a lad just turned
15, and promises, ere he is rauoh older, to perform well
off a shorter start than the limit (320yds) he wa* accorded.
Of individual riders throughout all races, Megson is with-
out a doubt the hero of the day. In addition to the mi'e,
he finished first in his Austral heat and first in his one
and a-half mile handicap heat; his run home in the lait-
named event, in which he had to pull up some 200yds
in the final lap, forcing applause from “even the ranks
of Tuscany.” Comic relief was provided by an obstao'e-
raoe in fancy costume and was welcome, for a couple of
the earlier accidents suggested at first something like
tragedy. The hlood-and-hair element, however, was en-
tirely absent, and the manner in which Qargurveitoh’s
trainer hauled his prostrate and apparently injured form
by the heels while its skull dragged with a series of grue-
some bumps along the track, was the one incident in the
afternoon’s sport which struck a responsive chord in the
hearts of the football-barraokers present.

Cycling and rowing seem to be allied sports.
Men riding at the Austral Meeting who are identified with
both: Neil Matterson, Chris. Neilson, Jack Fitzgerald, B.
Schultz, Kingsley and Peterson.

Dear Bulletin, —There was as much woe as
wheel at the Melb. Bicycle Club’s meeting last Saturday.
3000 people and 1000 deadheads hooted the championship
heats all round the course. The second heat did the mile
|n 3min. 8 3-sth secs.—about 19 miles an hour. There
was one flat race on the programme—and it was the
race all day that wasn’t flak The M. 8.0. ask for sugges-
tions to improve their show—l propose that at their next
sports they have no cycle races at all,—Respectfully, Y.Z.
• « • •
Re the running of Carnage when “ out of form.”
Tub Bulletin must admit, on second thoughts, and in
justice to Mr. W. R. Wilson, that by scratching Carnage
for the Cup, even after his wretched performance in the
Melb. Stakes, the owner would have gained nothing but a
little ignorant abuse. The horse carried a heap of winter-
wager money—plenty of Wilson money, too, for that
matter—and although the “party” at last abandoned
hope of a win with Nada’s stable-companion, there was
good reason for letting him have another go on the off-
chance. It may he likewise urged that Carnage wasn’t
"out of form,” in the general acceptance of the term.
The horse had been thoroughly prepared and was seem-
ingly fit to travel as fast as of yore. But a Derby winner
who, whilst in the beat of physical health, loses'the dis-
position to gallop, and can’t be induced to gallop at home
or abroad, is, from a practical point of view, the most
“ out of form ” beast that a punter drops his money on.
Granting that the case of Carnage is exceptional, Tub
Bulletin can still point to dozens of modern oases in
which Australian “crack” horses were pulled out for
races without being " fancied by the stable,” a practice
almost unknown among those English owners whose
colors are followed by the general public.
