probable he will leave Australia. Now, Sam Hordern 1
* * * •
The new bowler is always a “wonder” if he
happens to come off at the outset, but it is the old bowler
who, in an unsensational way, generally pans out best at
the finish. Howell’s 5 wickets for 44 against the English-
men (Ist innings) at Sydney overshadowed Peel’s modest
8 for 25 against N.S.W. But when the match was over
Peel had bagged 8 wickets for 139, and Howell’s average
had fallen to 5 for 61. The five test matches will prob-
ably prove that Turner is still the champion Australian
trundler.
• • • •
It is time that cricket authorities made a
sensible rule as to catches. A fieldsman who doesn’t hold
a catch for three seconds before tossing the ball up again
should be bound to hold it for the second time. If he
gets only a half grasp of the ball in the first instance
jerks it hastily into*the air and fails to grip it at the next
try, the " catch” should count as a miss. A fair catch is
always held long enough to establish its genuineness, and
the throwing up of the ball is a distinct afterthought.
Yet we have such stuff as this from newspaper reporters:
“ Richardson seemed to make a pretty catch and then
dropped the ball in the act of drawing back his hand to
throw it up,” or “ Farquhar caught the ball and threw it
on the ground.’’ The ball that is dropped or thrown
down (!) within a couple of seconds has never been held
at all.

Giffen and Lyons are now friends again. They
made it up over a glass of wine through the agency of
Major Wardill just before the match England v. S.A.

• * • «
South Australia won the Interprovincial cricket-
match by 10 wickets. Victoria made 252 on its first, and
134 on its second innings, a total of 386. Harry, who
made 35 and 45 on his two appearances, was the most
consistent performer ; Trott made 38 and 21; R. M’Leod
made 46 in his first innings and altogether failed to come
off in the second ; and Worrall, who scored 47 in the first
innings, went to pieces for 2in the next. The S.A. men
made 358 in one innings, Dyer’s 102 and Gillen's 94 not
out being the biggest features in the score. Giffen took
12 Victorian wickets altogether—in fact, the match con-
sisted mostly of Giffen in one form or another.
• * * •
The cricket match England against 22 of New
England at Armidale (N.S.W.) ended, through want of
time, in a draw. England made 67 in the first innings,
and 197 in the second, M’Laren (49) and Ford (44) being
top scorers. The local 22 had a long and melan-
choly record of blanks, but managed to compile
147 all told. Stumps were drawn a few’ minutes
after the beginning of New England’s second innings,
and the local men were thus rescued from an
unpromising situation. The Englishmen complain bit-
terly that playing against 22, in the ordinary acceptation
of the term, meant that their opponents were allowed 22
batsmen, but that only eleven should take the field, and
that the New Englanders, by sending their whole force
into the field, were running the thing into the ground.
Which was possibly true, but the question is open to a lot
of argument.

• • « •
Rowing clubs, like nations, have their rise,
youth, and fall, the ignominious defeat of the Yarra
Yarra in the Vic. Champion Fours being a notable
example. Five years ago this club formed, out of a
brilliant junior eight and three old senior men, a combin-
ation well-nigh invincible, and, for three seasons, it
carried off both the eights and fours with ease. But the
Man with the Scythe is invariably disregarded by all
athletes, who always deem themselves immortal and con-
sider that because Smith was a magnificent oar in ’B4 he
must necessarily be so in '94. This sentiment, coupled
with a liberal amount of Brotherbiliisra, would wreck a
crew of eight Stanburys, as it has wrecked the Y. Y., for a
time at least.

“ To keep a heavy tax on bicycles is to penalise
a considerable section of the community,” says the
Arous. Sweetly innocent, this, in the light of disclosures
made in a recent Customs case, showing that firms
charge £22 and £25 for bicycles that stand them in about
£7 10s.

The kerosene-launch, regarded as a means of
harbor locomotion, has only one disadvantage—its oily
perfume. If anyone would invent a kerosene-launch
which would smell of whisky instead of oil he ought to
make a fortune.
* * * •
When N.S.W. law pleases it does not recognise
betting. When it also pleases it does—peculiarly. At
Sydney Water Police Court a punter prosecuted a layer
for having obtained 6s. under false pretences. Layer
offered Bto 1 ; punter put up ss. and won. Layer offered
ss. back ; punter wanted 455. or nil. Hence the trouble.
Beak Edwards ruled that punter was only entitled to 55.,
and discharged layer, who was subsequently arrested
under the elastic Vag.

“Will eggs keep, packed in sawdust?’
asks a Leader correspondent. They ought to;
see how the average ballet girl keeps.
The oldest provincial paper in S.A. is the
Border Watch, first published at Mount Gambier
in 1861. It is a bi-weekly, and is still about the
best and most prosperous of the country sheets.
A loyal Southern contemporary, speak-
ing of a visit of the Duchess of Teck to a London
hospital, alludes to that “ great ” lady as a “ ray
of sunshine.’’ Her ladyship weighs 18st. in her
evening-dress. Fortunately, this _“ ray ’’ does not
seem to have fallen on any of the invalids.
A Melb. deadbeat having learned on a
Friday evening that four large boilers would next
day be temporarily deposited on a secluded piece
of land, went around the fraternity and let out
the sleeping accommodation at Id. a night—cash
'down—to some 30 others of the profession.
It was seriously proposed in the Queensland
Parliament that the State should take over the
Mt. Morgan gold. “It belongs to the State and
yet the State makes a present of it to certain indi-
viduals.” So said Jack Hamilton in supporting
the proposal. The men who voted against the
State paying members of Parliament a living
wage voted to continue giving Mt. Morganaires
all the gold they can take out of the land —with
the tiny deduction made by dividend-tax.
The “Dublin jackeen” wasoysteringalong
a foreshore in Middle Harbor, Sydney. “These
oysters are very small,” said he. “Yes, that’s so,’
said the old Cornstalk, “but y’see, we can’t afford
to feed ’em.” “D’you feed oysters?” “Of
course you do. They wanter be fed twice a day if
you’re fattening them for the market.” “ What
d’you feed ’em with?” “Oatmeal.” _“ Go on.
“ Where have you been to all your life and not
know that? You know Woodward, the big
Sydney oyster-grower. He has a great under-
ground plantation of ’em, and his man has to go
round with a big bucketful of their gruel twice a
day.” “But how does he feed ’em?” “ With a
big tablespoon; two tablespoonfuls to each
■oyster.” “But how does he get it into their
mouths?” “Why, you silly, they open their
shells directly they hear his footsteps, and if he
'happens to be a minute or two behind the regular
feed-time, there’s such a clapping with the open-
ing and shutting of shells that you’d think there
was a hallfull of nigger-minstrels playing the
ibones.” “Begorra, but it’s a wonderful place
; and the oyster’s a wonderful insect and bates all.”
So far, there are no pawn-shops or second-
Ihand dealers in Tonga, Samoa, Fiji, or
Hebriles, In any of these places, pawn-shops
\w mid no doubt be rushed, but no one would ever
ire leem a pledge or attend the auction sales. An
li dander, as a rule, does not treasure relics, and
ithe more pawn-tickets he or she could string
iround the neck or leg, the greater and mightier
ithe chief or chieftainess. The dealers would like-
wise be rushed, but they would hardly sell any-
thing, as the natives only like brand-new things.
The successors of Bray Bros., the well-
known George-street glass-merchants, advertise a
special clearing sale, at the lowest prices ever
known in Sydney. In their fine collection of
Parian statuary and terra-cotta figures are some
of the cheapest, choicest, chastest Christmas-boxes
imaginable.
Melbournian : “Merely walking over the
short block between Russell and Swanston-streets,
on one side, I yesterday counted four men and
four women, all selling War Ckys. These eight
strong people were uniformed officers, supported
by public subscription, and doing work which
would be better done by one small boy.”
The astonishing illness of a Sydney boot-
manufacturer is accounted for, and he is now
rapidly regaining his usual color. He turned
green on reading the testimonials to the extra-
ordinary success of Ellis’s self-measurement
system, under which boots unsurpassed for price,
fit, and quality are posted to any part of Aus-
tralasia from W. Ellis’s Intercolonial Boot
Factory, Haymarket, Sydney ; or Hunter-street,
Newcastle.
A medical item. On the N.S.W.-Q. bor-
der, lately, a man was bitten by a snake, and
after the doctor had injected ammonia he told the
patient's friends “not to let him go to sleep for a
bit, but to walk him about.” They did walk him
about—until the unfortunate was nearly dead
with fatigue. Then they led him by the nose with
hot pincers, while one behind pricked him gently
with a pitchfork. They kept this up one night
and half a day, and finally tied a cow-bell round
his neck. Yet the man survived both snakebite
and treatment.
The great-Australian-adjective yarn with
frills on : —The newchum parson was collecting
church-funds and a crowd of jokers pointed out to
him a big fellow leaning against the hotel veran-
dah. “ Try him. That’s Mr. Tyson, the million-
aire.” The poor shepherd bailed him up and
asked him to aid the cause. “ Look pa’son,
I ain’t Mr. gory Tyson, and I ain’t an Episco—
gory—palian, nor a Uni —gory —tarian, nor a
Congre—gory —gationalist. But I’m an inde—
gory—pendent se—crimson —lector from Bunge-
blessed —worgorai, and—hie I—cumanava drink.”
Photographers, as well as other people,
have good reason to rejoice and be exceeding glad
that Xmas only comes once a year. Any man,
woman, or young person smitten with the first
symptoms of the disease, to say nothing of the
large army of chronic sufferers therefrom, will do
wisely to resist and flee from the temptation of
even looking at the latest importation of new
goods just unpacked at Baker and Rouse’s ware-
house, 375 George-street. All the necessaries and
most of the luxuries pressed into the service of
the art are to be found there pretty well all the
year round, but the more recent additions to the
stock cover all the newest inventions and up-to-
date dodges, for soothing one’s friends or crucifying
enemies on a sensitised plate. Field and studio
cameras of every make and design, the thirty-five
shilling snap-shot for rapid and unerring execu-
tion by land or sea, brilliant finders, concentrated
energy in the shape of shutters and lenses by all
makers of renown, are just being drafted into their
respective corners after being branded with the
hieroglyphics of the firm. The only way to avoid
the discovery of what those characters implj
is to avoid the locality ; but for the curious the
address is Baker and Rouse, 375 George-street,
i Sydney.

(£150,000) CORPORATION
4 PER CENT. DEBENTURES.
The Municipal Council of the City of Brisbane is
now prepared to receive applications for the whole
or any portion of the Balance (<£150,000) of the
Debentures authorised by Act 57 Victoria, No. 13.
Interest at the rate of 4 per cent, per annum,
dating from issue of Debentures, will be payable
in Brisbane half-yearly.
Principal sum payable in Brisbane Ist January,
1919. Arrangements have been made whereby
the Union Bank of Australia will receive and
transmit moneys on account of above from any of
their offices in Australia at special rates.
Application forms obtainable from the Town
Clerk.
ROBERT FRASER, Mayor.
Town Hall, Brisbane, Oct. 16, 1894.

TWENTY POUNDS
out
Twenty Pence .
You’ve twenty pounds to spend—or twenty
pence, if you like, Your object is to get all you
require for the money you have, and you do not
desire even to contemplate the possibility of being
called upon for more. If you BUY OF US you
will get all you determined to secure, and have a
bit left for something else. Also, you will get the
best of everything—the goods of standard manu-
facturers. If you BUY ELSEWHERE your
TWENTY POUNDS, or your TWENTY
SHILLINGS, as the case may be, will certainly
not go so far as you thought; you will either have
to SINK THE QUALITY, and be content with
inferior goods, or
Shell out more Cash .
Will that suit you ? We trow not. You’re no
fool. You’ve been wandering up and down this
blessed world for years and know your book.
One may say, “This is only assertion; where’s
your proof?” We reply that even as the quality
of that popular mixture of various compounds and
substances known as a pudding is proved by its
effect upon the constitution of the person who
assimilates it, so are we prepared with sound and
lucid demonstration. We say—Use your eyes,
exercise your judgment, criticise and compare.
Set your mental machinery to work, and convey
to your mind, through the medium of your organs
of visions, and with the aid of those glorious
reasoning powers with which you have been en-
dowed, the impressions that will be yours, and
you’ll find engraved upon your heart of hearts the
name of

round the joints AA, round the instep 88, round heelCC,

The three successive scorchers, Sunday,
Monday, Tuesday, in Melb. last week, have not
been paralleled for 40 years. On two days it was
157 in sun, 105 shade.
Dear Bulletin, Your correspondent,
*' M, Rule,” who wrote to you about disem-
bowelling scrub cattle in C.Q. and N.fy, did not
tell you all. It is also a common practice there to
tango up alongside a beast in a thick scrub, out
enough hide from him to make a whip, plait it,
fix it on a handle, and drive the beast home with
it.—Yours, Another Liar.
A N.S.W. country correspondent :
Work is being turned out of N.S.W. Government Print-
ing Office in competition with country printers at shock-
ingly low prices. The Municipal Council here wanted 100
copies of their bye-laws (just gazetted). They asked me for
k price and obtained a price from the Govt, printer. They
contained 120,000 ens. Mr. Potter did the 100 copies for
15s. (fifteen shillings), which would just about pay for
the binding and cutting. Why Govt, should be called
upon to print bye-laws for a council at a dead loss, is
beyond my ken.
Cable : “ On the fall of Port Arthur, the
Japanese captured Reuter’s special reporter on the
ground that he was acting in the service of the
Chinese.” The Japs are not so civilised as the
Turks. During the last Rnsso-Turkish war AH
Pasha bamboozled the London newspaper corres-
pondents by ostentatiously preparing to throw a
bridge over the Danube. They reported the fact,
and 50,000 Russians were kept idle for a month
waiting for the expected crossing.
B. Mountcastle and Sons, of 332 George-
street, Sydney, who are sole agents for Cooksey’s
London hats, have laid in an extra quantity of
these celebrated belltoppers for the Christmas
season. John Bull’s belltopper is his god ; as h«
plods through his old, grimy, fog-stricken capital
he stops every half-mile at a hatter’s to have it
ironed ; he worships the nap on it, and yearns
with a holy fervour over the beautiful curliness of
its brim. Therefore, the bell-topper naturally
reaches its highest perfection in London, and the
fact that Cooksey’s hats are celebrated even there
is a sufficient proof of their magnificence. Mount-
castle and Sons also deal in white troppo helmets,
warranted sun and rain proof; straw hats of the
male and female variety ; ladies’ sailor hats, Terai
bats and puggarees, and every other description
■that a civilised community can possibly ask for.
'Further, they are manufacturers as well as im-
porters, and so the man whose abnormal head
•can’t be suited with the ready-made article can
have his brain measured and his hat made on the
premises.
Editor Bulletin, —.Be your article on the
“Wairarapa wreck.” Years ago, Capt. MTntosh
had charge of a steamer in the South Seas. Late
one afternoon, when quite close to the passage
that levds through a certain coral reef, a hurricane
came suddenly from seaward, and the rolling of
the breakers on the reef was so heavy that an
unbroken line of foam reached across ahead of the
ship. Darkness coming on, the entrance could
not be seen. The vessel was too close in to steam
round and be brought head-on to it, so all the
captain could do was to go full-speed astern for
eight or nine hours till the wind abated. The
poor engineer completely lost his bead with
fright, and raced round the deck Bible in hand.
Mac said to him, “ Why don’t you behave like a
man, and not alarm the passengers?” He refused
to be pacified, so the captain seized him, rushed
him into his room, and locked him there and went
to his place again on the bridge ; but the old
iellow bashed away at the door until he had to be
let out. I mention this to show that in the pre-
sence of danger. Captain MTntosh was not a man
likely to lose his presence of mind. Re your
“Significant Facts ’’ in another column ; they are
not so significant, after all, and I’ll tell you why.
The crew of a ship are used to a sea-life and its
dangers ; passengers are not. The crew are used
to tlie ship, and can find their way about as well
at night as in the day-time ; the passengers are all
strange to the ship—most likely some had never
been on deck all the passage—and when the
moment of danger arrived were completely lost
and knew not the best place to make for. The
shipwrecked woman was right when she said,
“ God help the poor passengers : the sailors are
used to it.”— Shellback.

“Ghosts”: And its Critics.
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondents “ Walter
Clay ” and “ Arthur Bray ” have fallen into the
very common error of instituting comparisons
between the productions of the poets. How can
you compare poems? Lovers of poetry are like
drinkers «f alcohol. Of the latter, some like long
draughts of light wine or beer, others strong
wines out of small glasses, others, still, the fiercer
brandy or whisky. Now, which of the above
drinks is best ? As one who likes to hear “the
canakin clink ” whenever it holds any sort of
decent liquor, I answer all are good if pure.
Similarly, the “pure gold of poetry” must be
accepted by the world as current coin, no matter
into what shape or of what size it may have
been minted. “Walter Clay” likes brevity—
the poetic “nip”—others love the poetic
“schooner.” Who would blot out one line of
“Childe Harold” because of its length? “Ghosts”
is a fine poem, and so is “ Crossing the Bar.” And
both Gordon and Kendall are poets. Although in
many respects imperfectly educated, I, like Long-
fellow’s young Sicilian, have “read the poets o’er
and o’er,” and I apply a test to their productions,
which several illustrious men have assured me is,
in their opinion, a perfectly natural and correct
one : If the mention, oral or written, of a writer’s
name at once calls to my mind something that I
have considered striking in his poems, I always
hold him to be a true poet. The mere mention of
Longfellow, whom “ Arthur Bray ” puts out of
court so summarily, conjures up a mental picture
of how —
The grey alligator glided
Into the still bayou.
I never knew that the not very famous David
Christie Murray was a poet till I read in The Bul-
letin “The Meditations of Michael Tergovitch—
Bound for the Gallows.” Since that time the
sight of Murray’s name in a newspaper brings to
my lips the lines commencing—
Let the snake live out her damned ophidian life,
And rear the little brood she loves ;
Were I a snake there should be some insistance on snake-
rights, &c., &c.,
and for me Murray has become one of the Im-
mortals.
I think it wrong to use the terms “greater
poets,” “minor poets.” If a man is a poet his
metrical productions are poetry in the same sense
that pure gold is gold refined. Some poets crush
a larger yield than others, or, to pursue my pre-
vious metaphor, have a larger poetic brew, but
comparisons cannot be made. Their productions
are true poetry or nothing. As for those who try
to be poets and cannot achieve the distinction —
failing to beat the music out— their dirgeis written:
that his lately-published “The Dead Infant ” is a
perfect poem in the same sense that “ Ghosts ” is
a fine poem. The lines —
Alas for those who never sing,
But die with all their music in them.
Just a last word in favor of Victor Daley, since
the poem of the obscure Ropes has been given
deserved prominence. I may point out the fact
The foolish hopes and fond
That cheat us to the last
Thou heedest not, beyond
All these things thou hast passed
are from the true poetic ore-shoot, and confirm the
opinion that I, with many others, have long held
—that Daley is a true poet. Consider the whole
poem well and mark how the slightest trace of the
Sunday-school mawkishness, which is almost
always present in verse treating of this and
kindred subjects, is not discoverable. The gentle
cadence of this beautiful poem seems like a softly-
murmured answer from “a high hidden land” to
the question, “Is it well with the child ? ” “It is
well.” Let us take heed that we native lovers of
poetry who profess to be ever on the watch for
the advent of our poets, do not overlook the one
who dwells in our midst while he is yet here. —
Yours, Jerome Darnet, Gould’s Country, Tas,
Dear Bulletin, —You don’t quite give the
congratulatory telegrams sent by Purves to bank-
manager Cotton, and Sleath, M.L.A., to Surgeon
O’Hara. Purves’s wire is said to have been :
“Well done, Cotton. Hit the brute in a softer
place next time.” When Purves was bashed,
Sleath repeated it word for word, only changing
the name. It reminds one of Napoleon 111. and
Francis Joseph of Austria. In ’66 the latter asked
Napoleon to help him against the Prussians.
Napoleon answered : “Do you think I will ally
myself with a corpse?” In '7O Napoleon, after
his first defeats, asked help from Austria. Francis
Joseph answered: “Do you think I will ally
myself with a madhouse?” —X.X.

THE MIGHT HAVE ESCAPED ANY DAY.
' There was once a man, who, on account of his
i religious convictions, was arrested by a despotic
government and thrown into prison. Looking at
the strong stone walls that shut him in, he natur-
ally concluded that he must spend the rest of his
days in this lonely and dismal place. Yet wretched
as the prospect was, there was some comfort in the
thought that he was a victim of oppression, and
• not undergoing punishment as a violator of any
good and righteous law. All the same, he would
• have been glad to get out, as he had a right to do
if he could.
Nevertheless, there he stayed and languished for
sixteen years. At nearly the end of that period
it occurred to him one day to climb up on a
projecting stone in the wall and take a peep
through the window above his head. He did so,
and found the iron bars removable and the sash
not fastened. An idea struck him, of course.
Why not escape ? He opened the window, jumped
six feet to the ground, and left that district
immediately. He might have done so at any time
during his protracted occupancy of the place, if he
had only been blessed with a more inquiring mind
and not taken so much for granted.
Now here comes an incident both like and unlike
(that of the martyr ; like it because there was loss
•of free action, unlike it because the sufferer was
mot imprisoned as be was.
The story is in the form of a letter, one of those
betters we all prefer to get—short and made up of
•short words. The writer, a lady, says : “For the
ilas't sixteen years I have been greatly afflicted
'with bilious complaints and weakness. I was
always tired, weak, and low-spirited. I had no
appetite, and when food was placed before me
■I could not touch it. For days I could scarcely
-eat anything. At times I was troubled with
sickness and heaving at the stomach, spitting up a
sthvck phlegm. I had a troublesome cough, with
dpain at my chest and difficulty of breathing. I
•got very weak and could scarcely drag myself in
and out of the shop. In December, 1891, I began
to take a medicine I had heard well spoken of by
many of my customers, called Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. After I had taken only a few
doses I felt relief, and I had not used the medicine
for a fortnight when I felt better than I had done
for years. Thus encouraged, I kept on with it
until I was restored to good health. My son,
Arthur, had suffered off and on for a year from
pain and heaviness after meals, with a sinking
feeling at the pit of the stomach. He was induced
to try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and the
result was the same as in my own case. I know
several persons in this district who have been
cured by the Syrup after all other means had
failed. You are at liberty to publish my letter if
you think it might be of benefit to others afflicted
as I was. —Yours truly, (signed) (Mrs.) M. Ward,
174, Ilkeston-road, Radford, Nottingham, April
Ist,. 1892.”
We congratulate Mrs. Ward upon her deliver-
ance from a distressing and dangerous disease.
What a pity she did not sooner learn how easily
and certainly she could be cured. Sixteen years
is too large a slice out of one’s life to be spent in
bodily and mental misery. Very few of us can
have wealth in any case, but health should be a
blessing common and universal as sunshine. And
it might be if everybody knew how to preserve it
when they possess it, or to regain it when lost.
This lady’s disease was of the digestive organs—
•indigestion and dyspepsia—the most afflictive and
perilous of all, because it involves every function of
the body, scattering its poison at every point
through the medium of the blood. Nearly all
ailments are but the differently-named results and
symptoms of this prolific parent of evil. That the
remedy site mentions should have cured her will
not surprise those who know its well-founded
reputation. Many worse cases constantly yield
to it.
Our friend the martyr abode in his cell a weary
time because he was ignorant of the simple way
out. If the reader of these lines is a captive to
disease he has no excuse for remaining so. In her
short and honest letter our correspondent points
to the open door.
JACK CADE.
A TRIBUTE TO THE MEMORY OF A MUCH-MALIGNED PATRIOT.
(See “ Henry VI." Second Part. Act IV. Scene X.)
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Bard Divine, whose fame like daylight
Over all the earth is spread !
Shakespeare, whose bright name doth ray light
Through the dusk of ages dead !
Thou oouldst paint a portrait rarely,
Yet, in sooth, I am afraid
That thy pencil wrought unfairly
On the picture of Jack Cade.
Here, where old gum-trees funereal.
Like to ancient tatter’d rakes,
Shake and sigh—a scene ideal
For thy melancholy Jaques*—
In a land where Puck for pleasure
May, while girdling earth, have stood ;
I am reading thee at leisure,
In a calm judicial mood.
And I think thy mediseval
Social views suit not this clime—
There has been a great Upheaval
Of the Masses since thy time.
When thy pen, like claw of tercel,
Tore the Early Democrat —
Was it proof that Universal
Wisdom lodged beneath thy hat?
Here I pause—in time to shun light
Words of treason ’gainst a king.
Thou with vision strong as sunlight
Wert not blind to one sole thing.
No ! In manner Rabelaisian—
Eulogy ironical —
Thou didst suit to the occasion,
And the age, thy words withal.
This belief I shall abide in.
Holding it I am content
To accept with calmness Iden,
Huge beef-witted ’Squire of Kent;
And can find excuse for pardon
For the ill mind he betrayed,
Gasconading in his garden
O’er the corpse of poor Jack Cade.
’Tis a scene my fancy oft in
Idle hours presents to me,
The quaint, old-world garden-croft in
Kent, the county of the bee.
And while thus my thoughts run riot
Figures rise mine eyes before-
Champions that have lain full quiet
These four hundred years and more.
Cade, the famished little phantom
With lords’ blood upon his hands.
Once again, as bold as bantam
Cock in Iden’s leek-bed stands,
And, with proud, pugnacious air crows,
Though, unkempt and gaunt, he looks
Like a General of Scarecrows
Beaten in a war with Rooks.
Hear him : “ Like an ostrich, iron
Thou shall eat; and like a pin
My sword swallow.” To admire an
Elf like this is, sure, no sin—
“ Meat these five days I’ve not eaten,
Yet come five, and, by the Mass,
If I leave ye not dead-beaten—
May I never more eat grass !”
Hear the other, who took pride in
The high honor in him bred—
Alexander Ajax Iden—
-“Now it never shall be said
An Esquire of Kent ” —a wombat!—
“ Took such odds—it shall not be !
A poor, famished man to combat,
Pray observe the size of me !
“ Look ! my foot, and arm, and leg are
Thrice as big as thine, poor thief !
Why, thou starving little beggar,
I am bold and full of beef
Little danger ran he, being
What he was, with his great blade
Swung by brawn-fed arm—in freeing
“The unconquered soul of Cade.”
See how Iden, fiercely loyal,
Hies to Court for his reward,
Hot-foot, hungering for royal
Blessing on his bloody sword !
Small tho fear that he Would tarry,
When Cade’s head in hand he bore—
Sure he never had to carry
Such a weight of brains before I
By his Sovereign he was knighted
In the fashion of the day,
And went home to Kent delighted.
To improve his backsword play. ...
Democrats ! Mark well the sequel!
Cade your protomartyr was,
And he fought that fight unequal
For your Order and your Cause.
Still you fight, as erstwhile Cade did
Fight, and hunger conquers you!
But not always God-unaided
Shall they fight whose cause is true.
Therefore, fill your glasses fairly,
Though the toast be long delayed,
We shall drink it, standing squarely—
“ To his memory—Jack Cade !”
VICTOR J. DALEY.

ANARCHISM
UP TO DATE.
The average person’s conception of Anarchism is
of a piece with his other conceptions—a compound
of selfishness, prejudice, and ignorance. These
qualities form the sole basis of the judgments of a
large number of “ respectable ” people—from the
laborer turned employer, who- becomes the worst-
tyrant of the class from which he sprang, to the
politician who plunders the public treasury to
secure the return to Parliament of bis own par-
ticular pal. These gentry. The Bulletin’s Fat
Men, the typical bourgeois, while cheating one
another in the name of Business and saeri ficing
the proletariat in the name of Law and Order,
unite in blind, unreasoning hatred of any move-
ment which seems, to . threaten the fruit of their
lying, cozening, bullying, and thieving, and
present in, every age a solid opposition to schemes
of progress, which, aim at lessening social in-
equalities. If they have the capacity to realise-
the difference between various plans for the
amelioration of the condition.of the masses, they
rarely, trouble to use it. All new ideas seem to-
them but confused versions-of the great idea of
Change, which they, are at all hazards engaged to-
combat. Views of abstract justice or injustice,,
right or wrong, have no place in their philosophy.
“Justice” is to them a machine which they
employ to bawl “ Sedition!” “Blasphemy!” “ Dis-
loyalty !” “ Sacrilege !” and similar cant-words at
the mildest dissenters from conventional beliefs.
“Right” is their power to imprison those dis-
senters, to .flog them, and finally to hang them;,
while “ Religion,” in grotesque mockery, salves
possible qpalms with the assurance that a bourgeois-
God, propitiated ty the homage of the collection-
plate, will adjust any errors in “ mansions beyond!
the aides.”
» * «
It is not surprising, then, to find an ex-Minister
of N.S. Wales lumping “Socialism, Anarchism,
Communism,” as similar objects of detestation in i.
a recent burst of aristocratic invective. Yet any-
body acquainted merely with the dictionary--'
meaning of the words should know that Socialism
and Anarchism are as far asunder as Repub-
licanism and knighthood; and that, at least-
historically, Communism is a very
thing from either. But the utterance was*;
characteristic of the bourgeoisie, which does not
take the trouble to understand anything, to
acquaint itself with anything, but thinks with its
belly and foams into furious speech whenever
instinct teaches it that its belly is in danger. It
is not helped when somebody points out that
Socialism means the extension of the sphere of
government and the regimentation of society to a
much greater extent than at present, while
Anarchism means the destruction of all govern-
ment, and insists, with Diderot, that Nature made
neither servant nor master, and laws should
neither be imposed nor obeyed. The bourgeois
intelligence has no trouble in assailing contraries,
even when considered merely as ideals, with the
same vituperation. “ There were once,” says
Hans Andersen, “ three green peas who lived in a
pod. They were green, and the pod was green,
and they thought all the world was green.”
Similarly, the bourgeoisie is mean, and it lives on
a mean, and it decrees that all the world must
limit aspirations to its mean standard. The
expectation is vain.
• • •
In contradistinction to Socialism, which preaches
duty to the State, Anarchism preaches duty to
one’s-self. The contest is the old contest between
altruism and egoism, with the proviso that the
altruism is mingled with a good deal of selfishness
and the egoism is characterised rather by a denial
of selfishness. The Socialist says, in effect,
“ Help others, that others may help you.’’ The
Anarchist prefers to say, “Be yourself, and you
will help others.” The motto of Anarchism
might indeed be Polonius’ precept to Laertes :
This above all—to thine ownself be true ;
And it must follow, as the night the day,
Thou canst not then be false to any man.
THE FAT MAN.—The True Leprosy.
The conception of the anarchist is undoubtedly
the higher. Socialism is in essence compulsory,
Anarchism is voluntary ; and the view of a human
society which considers its members as merely
specialised parts of a huge machine, and exacts
from every individual abject obedience to the
mandates of a central authority, is less noble,
less exalted than the view which holds that society
should be merely the optional co-operation of in
dependent units, each conscious of complete free*
dom to do as he pleases, yet so self-controlled ..s
to do only actions not conflicting with the freedom
of others. This is the ideal evolutionary
anarchists, who hopeor believe that in the gradual
progress of humanity the leading-strings of law
will by-and-by become unnecessary; and, whatever
our sense of the difficulties in the of realisa-
tion, it must remain the ideal for which, beyond
all others, it is best for humanity to strive.
• * *
Enlightened anarchists recognise that their
millennium cannot be brought about by force, but
must await the slow process of events. They hold
that the gradual diffusion of correct ideas, and
the removal by degrees of the old savage leaven of
selfishness, are the only things from which per-
manent improvement is to be expected. They
have learnt from history the lesson that
force implies recoil, and that the last state
of a society unprepared for sudden change
may be worse than the first. But with
the anarchist movement, as with all others,
is associated a vanguard of hasty and impatient
spirits who are not content with the slow advance
of evolution, “Anarchy might be postponed to
the 30th century,” say these, “if we didn’t break
a little glass now and again. A man can’t move
ahead without shifting his legs, and revolutions
shift the legs of society. They turn things upside
down for a while, but when the ruins are so fertile
it is pusillanimous to dread them.’’ This, of
course, is a direct defence of the bomb. Evolu-
tionists, on the other hand, say that “government
by bomb would be the worst conceivable form of
government, an intangible despotism, destitute
even of that cynical audacity which leads the
despot to brave openly the fury he excites. The
strictest respect for human life must incontestably
be the basis of any system of social ethics. The
bomb is the direct antithesis of Anarchism, and
the veritable anarchist doctrine has the more
reason to deprecate it because up to now it is the
anarchist agitation that has suffered the most
from its introduction.”
• • •
Language like this is dubbed rose-water reason-
ing by the revolutionary anarchists who count as
martyrs Ravachol, Vaillant, and Caserio. Their
creed is summed up in “ Father Duchesne’s
Song,” sung by Ravachol on the way to the
guillotine. This famous chant, which The Bul-
letin now ventures to translate, runs as follows :
This hymn of Anarchy (taken togetherwi'ffi
the pictures here reproduced from the sup-
pressed organ of French anarchism, “Le Pi?i n-
Peinard) gives a very adequate idea of tie-
spirit which European Governments are trying:
to stifle by an execution or two. It is m
spirit as old as authority, and it has found vent inn

Boys, my name is Pbre Duchesne,
Yes, by God !
Born in ’92 to pain.
Yes, by God 1
Marat made the music play—
You who hate his tune to-day,
Harkee 1 I’ve a word to say,
Yes, yes, by God !
Cowards, scoundrels, blackguards, thieves 1

Dawn to dusk I bind your sheaves.

Yours the ears all golden bright,
Mine the kennel-straw at night;
We’re “ the mob," you say—all right
Yes, yes. by God 1
And the skies are ours, they swear I

Pends ton proprietaire

In their faces spit, and then.
Having spat once, spit again E

numerous historical rebellions and revolutions.
Anarchism is only a new name, not a new thing ;
but Governments can never from experience.
Their first thought is always to crush down op-
position, no matter how well justified or how
solidly based. Nine times in ten they succeed,
thanks to the ad vantages given them by the pos-
session of power and by the generally conservative
tendencies of the mass of any population; the
tenth time, to their surprise, they find their
authority torn to shreds and themselves extin-
guished with something less of formality than it
has been their custom to concede to opponents.
Yet every new Government, as soon as it
feels itself firmly planted, proceeds in the same
fashion to weight down the safety-valve a
the first sound of escaping steam, and is ii,
variably astonished when, sooner or late
comes the inevitable explosion. Once gover V
ments learn that their safety lies in redressing;,
not in repressing, grievances, the seed of revolution
will never germinate again. But while they
regard it as their duty to fortify monopoly, to
maintain tyranny, to perpetuate class privileges ;
while they are content that the sweets of life fall
always to those who sit in the sunshine at the top
of the tree, and its bitter lees to those who sweat
iin the shadow at the root; so long will the grim
spectre of Anarchy stand always at their feasts.
Modern anarchy is intensified by neurotic civilisa-
tion, and made more dangerous by the destructive
powers which civilisation provides. But its
essence, now, as of old, is the divine instinct of
equality—equality of opportunity if not of reward,
the divine craving for freedom, the divine hatred
if injustice. The effects of revolutionary anarchy
—the conspiracies, the bomb-throwings, the assass-
inations—are indeed execrable. But what of its
causes—starvation, fraud, cruelty, tyranny, ini-
quity unspeakable—would these be less hateful if
custom Lad not made them so familiar?
# # *
Felix Dubois, in “ Le Peril Anarchiste ” (trans-
lated into English by Ralph Derechef, and pub-
lished by Fisher Unwin), gives a striking summary
of the history and tendencies of modern Anarch-
ism. As an active, organised force, it is not yet
20 years old ; but it has already put forth an
immense propaganda, and thoroughly alarmed
•the Latin nations of Europe. The German races,
more patient, characteristically sluggish, do not
take kindly to Anarchism. It has made no pro-
gress in England, and little in Germany, where
Socialism seems much more popular. The Slavs
*eem to owe their Nihilism less to choice
than to the necessity of making an effective
protest against despotic rule in Russia. Yet
Bakounine, a Russian (1814-76) was the father
of Anarchisn (This remarkable man personi-
fied the genius of discontent. He had an
astonishing influence over men and minds, and,
although intellectually inferior to Karl Marx, he
rivalled and eventually overthrew by sheer force
of personality Marx’s supremacy with the Inter-
national Association of Workers—the great
socialistic organisation which flourished most dis-
tinctively in the third quarter of this century.
But only after Bakounine’s death were anarchist
theories embodied in a formal declaration of doc-
trine- This has two sides : a negative and a posi-
tive. $ .The first says that everything must be
done away with. No more property : war to
capital, to privileges of all kinds, and to
the exploitation of man by man. No more
fatherland : no frontiers, no strife between
people and people. No more State: war to all
authority, elected or not, dynastic or temporary,
monarchs or Parliaments. And to make up for
this sweeping destruction, perfect liberty. The
social revolution is to have no other object than to
create a state of things in which everybody will
have nothing to do but look after himself. From
this result the three axioms of Anarchy :
No more anything.
Do as you -please.
Every thing's everybody's.
It would be hard to find three compactor maxims,
or three appearing more satisfactory to the Have-
Nots, more outrageous to the Haves.
THE GENIUS OF PATRIOTISM.
“Horrible wretch, she devours her own children!”
As long as anarchists confined themselves to
talking about their creed, European Governments
were little alarmed. Even now, as already ex-
plained, the more respectable and perhaps the
more considerable section of anarchists is not pre-
pared to do more than propagate its opinions by
writing and speech. But speech and writing are
all too tardy vehicles of progress for the more
violent section, so from Italy in 1876 came the
declaration that it was time to proceed to propa-
ganda by deed. From then till now, not a year
has passed without several attempts to awaken by
insurrection “the sleeping conscience of the
masses”—whom anarchists take for granted
will be on their side once they understand
what the trouble is about. The culmination of
that policy was reached only this year in the
assassination of President Carnot. That, from
the point of view of the propagandists by deed,
is the best thing Anarchism has yet accom-
plished. Carnot was killed not as a man, but as a
type —the type of that bourgeois class which turns
a deaf ear to the cry of the labourer until it is
startled into mercy, or terrified into justice. From
the point of view of modern society all such actions
must be in the highest degree criminal, and
enlightened philosophy, if it explains them, can-
not but condemn them. It should be the part of
thinkers and statesmen in Australia to see that the
tendencies to crime latent in every community
have not the shadow of justification in the condi-
tions of life and labour here. For, as Lacassngne
says, “Every society has the criminals which it
deserves. ”
The Jap is fastidious. He must have his
hot-water bath daily. He won’t work beside
kanakas. He hankers after indoor employment.
Lastly, his rice must be pure Japan, otherwise he
goes on strike, as he did a few weeks ago on a
certain Northern plantation.
Spagnoletti: “I have been attending a
send off, in Melbourne, of the ‘He’s a Jolly Good
Fellow ’ order. Gentlemen were told to charge
their glasses with a bumper, and did so, but
nobody dared to drink it. Here is a symptom of
the prevalent malaise. The business men are pretty
well all invalids, through the tension and worry.
What has become of the six-bottlers of yore ? ”
One Bengovey, of Melb., is a pathetic
instance of man’s willingness to lay down his life,
if necessary, in Beauty’s cause. Bengovey, ac-
cording to divorce-court evidence, has long been a
devout worshipper at the shrine of Mrs. Johnson
when her husband was off the premises, and his
love never flinched before the awful possibility of
Johnson’s unexpected arrival. If perchance
Johnson caught his gallant rival in a tender
moment he invariably kicked him on a tender
spot, after which he would spit at his pale face
and “rub in” the saliva. He also made a prac-
tice of bashing the intruder’s head against the
wall, as an inducement for him to put up his
hands and fight, but Bengovey preferred to spare
Johnson for the lady’s sake. Evidently she
admired her lover’s reposeful manner, for he con-
tinued to return like the prodigal to the fatted
calf that was ever at his disposal. The final
interview between Bengovey and Mr. Johnson
occurred in a bedroom. Bengovey had his coat
off at the time, and when he saw the husband
coming in to disturb the dark seance he sat down,
refusing all invitations to stand up and take his
usual hiding in the old familiar style. Johnson,
therefore, “gave him the hiding sitting down,
and then kicked him out,” retaining the coat as
spoils of victory. Mrs. Johnson got notice to
quit about the same time. A decree nisi, with
costs against the persevering co-respondent, was
granted last week, so (Bengovey is already in a
position to enjoy Mrs. Johnson’s society without
thinking of the hour when the bridegroom cometh.
Does the emancipated Lothario feel the happier
for that ? The Bulletin has doubts. He misses
the rapture of hearing his goddess enquire from
time to time—“ Tell me, darling, did he hurt you
much ?”

How Long ?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Through scopes of sky and under
The deeps of eartli and sea,
My soul went out in wonder,
To find but mystery —
A homeless stormbird flying
Through dim, dark seas uncrying,
Till spent with fruitless trying,
It came again to me ;
To find its old home dreary
Because all things had changed,
And all old ways were weary
For that its wings had ranged
O’er leagues of sea unsounded,
Through skies by clouds unrounded,
Through fields of space unbounded,
In ways from wings estranged.
But, ere its final turning,
It felt, in frowning skies,
The deathless glory burning-
. In Wisdom’s wond’rous eyes ;
That they who see shall never
Find rest again for ever,
Till time at last shall sever
The grain, from chaff of lies.
Mine eyes go forth to seaward,
In mute expectancy,
To skyward, or to leeward,
To find some hope for me;
The sullen skies are stormy,
And waves and winds before me,
I curse the times that bore me,
And dream of times to be.
I have grown tired of living
A life that no one knows ;
I sing no glad thanksgiving
While men are weighed with woes;
I know the winds are blowing,
I know the tide is flowing,
I know that Truth is growing,
Through all her thousand throes.
I know that earth the mother
Shall be to all men free,
That each will own the other,
In days that yet shall be ;
I hear like maidens singing
In leafy splendours swinging,
The slow years homeward bringing
The fruits of earth and sea.
But now, like sad seas sobbing.
’Gainst bold, black headlands strong,
I hear the world’s woes throbbing
Against the shores of Wrong,
That shake above and under
When Freedom’s tempests thunder—
I hear these things and wonder
How long, 0 God ! how long ?
Brisbane.
FRANCIES KENNA

•‘Evert man can write at least- one book,”every
man with brains has at least one good story to
tell; every man with or without brains moves in
.a different circle and knows things unknown to
any other man. Write carefully and plainly on
one side of the paper only, obliterating every un-
necessary word; then mail your work to The
Bulletin, which pays for accepted matter. In
certain of the so-called prize-competitions the
great prizes fall to the newspaper-proprietor, who
gelsthe guileless public to w ork for him for noth-
ing. Short Stories, or ballads, especially on bush,
mining, sporting,’ social, or dramatic themes, are
'preferred by The Bulletin ; 1360 words go to a
column. If you can possibly keep your story
within a column all the better Don’t write a
column on any subject if a half-column will do ;
don’t write half-a-colunin where a mere paragraph
is enough. “ Boil it down.”
